Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner
6 min
Permissible Transactions and Speech During I'tikaf
States permissible actions: buying/selling without goods, and speech; dislikes silence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on buying/selling during i'tikaf and explain why silence is disliked.

Core Idea
Trading without goods is allowed; silence is disliked because it mimics non-Islamic asceticism.

Many assume that i'tikaf requires complete withdrawal from worldly activities, but the Hanafi school permits necessary trade under specific conditions. This lesson clarifies what is allowed and what is disliked, based on the text of Al-Quduri and supporting hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
The passage states two rulings: buying/selling in the mosque is permissible (la ba's), and silence is disliked (yukrahu). Determine the hukm level for each.
la ba's means mubah; yukrahu means makruh tanzih.
2
Locate the condition for trade
Trade is allowed only if the commodity (al-sil'ah) is not brought into the mosque. Note that the prohibition on bringing goods is to preserve the sanctity of the mosque.
The Arabic phrase 'min ghayri an yuhdira al-sil'ah' sets this condition.
3
Understand the ruling on speech
The mu'takif should speak only good (khayr). Silence is disliked because it resembles the practice of non-Muslim ascetics and neglects the encouraged remembrance and beneficial speech.
Silence (al-samt) is not a form of worship in i'tikaf.
4
Connect to the proof
The Hanafi position is supported by the hadith of Aisha where the Prophet spoke with his wife during i'tikaf, showing that speech is permissible and silence is not required.
The hadith is from Sunan Abi Dawud; its chain is ahad (zanni al-thubut).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا بَأْسَ أَنْ يَبِيعَ وَيَبْتَاعَ فِي الْمَسْجِدِ مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ يُحْضِرَ السَّلْعَةَ وَلَا يَتَكَلَّمُ إِلَّا بِخَيْرٍ وَيُكْرَهُ لَهُ الصَّمْتُ.
There is no harm in his selling and buying in the mosque without bringing the commodity (into the mosque). He should not speak except good, and silence is disliked for him.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage clarifies that a mu'takif may engage in trade (sell or buy) within the mosque, provided he does not physically bring the merchandise into the mosque. The Hanafi school permits this as a necessary concession for those with economic hardship, as noted in the usul note. However, the mu'takif should only speak what is good (khayr); remaining silent (samt) is makruh (disliked) because it is a form of asceticism not prescribed in i'tikaf. The proofs include the practice of the Companions and the hadith of Aisha where the Prophet spoke with her during i'tikaf.

Key Terms
لَا بَأْس (la ba's): There is no harm; permissible
السَّلْعَة (al-sil'ah): The commodity/goods being traded
الصَّمْت (al-samt): Silence (refraining from speech)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a mu'takif remaining completely silent throughout his i'tikaf?
A) Permissible (mubah)
B) Recommended (mustahabb)
C) Disliked (makruh)
D) Prohibited (haram)

Key Takeaway
Buying/selling without goods is allowed; silence is disliked because the mu'takif should engage in beneficial speech.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Contracting Sale by Offer and Acceptance in Past Tense
States the foundational ruling that sale is valid only with past-tense offer and acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the required tense for sale contracts and apply it to distinguish valid from invalid expressions.

Core Idea
A sale is only valid when both offer and acceptance are in the past tense — anything else is a mere promise.

In Hanafi contract law, the precise wording of offer and acceptance determines the legal effect. This passage from Al-Quduri establishes the fundamental rule that a sale contract is concluded exclusively through past-tense expressions. Understanding this rule is essential for distinguishing binding contracts from non-binding promises.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and identify the act
The ruling is that a sale (bay') is contracted by offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) only when both are in the past tense (lafz al-madi). Identify the act as the contract of sale and the ruling as its validity conditional on past-tense wording.
The past tense indicates immediate and definitive consent.
2
Examine the primary dalil: the Quranic verse
The verse 'And Allah has permitted sale and forbidden usury' (2:275) is cited to establish the general legality of sale. The Hanafi school derives from this that a valid sale requires proper contractual structure, including specific wording (past tense) to differentiate from gifts or promises.
The Quran establishes the permissibility; the specific form is derived from Sunnah.
3
Analyze the hadith evidence
Five hadiths are presented. The first is the prohibition of harm (no harm and no mutual harm) implying that valid contracts prevent harm. The second is the written contract of al-Adda' ibn Khalid using past tense 'ishtara'. The third is the auction of the Prophet where he used 'bā'a' (past tense). The fourth is the sale of a camel where the Prophet used command 'bi'ni' but the response was past tense 'bā'a'. The fifth is from Ibn Umar using past tense. These establish Prophetic practice of using past tense in sales.
The consistent use of past tense by the Prophet (peace be upon him) is a key proof.
4
Consider the dispute
The Hanafi position is contested by other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) who hold that any expression indicating consent, including present or future tense, can form a valid sale. The Hanafi school argues that past tense is required to avoid ambiguity and ensure immediate obligation.
The thamarah al-khilaf: a sale in present tense is invalid in Hanafi but valid in Shafi'i.
5
Apply the rule to examples
Test: 'I sold my car to you' (past) → valid sale. 'I sell my car to you' (present) → not a contract, only a promise. 'I will sell my car' (future) → promise. Distinguish based on tense.
The mufta bihi position is that only past tense forms a binding contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كِتَابُ الْبُيُوعِ
قَالَ - رَحِمَهُ اللهُ -: الْبَيْعُ يَنْعَقِدُ بِالْإِيجَابِ وَالْقَبُولِ إِذَا كَانَا بِلَفْظِ الْمَاضِي.
وَجْهُ (1): الْآيَةُ لِثُبُوتِ ذَلِكَ: ﴿وَأَحَلَّ اللَّهُ الْبَيْعَ وَحَرَّمَ الرِّبَا﴾ (سورة البقرة، 257)
وَجْهُ (2): الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ ذَلِكَ: عَنْ أَبِي سَعِيدٍ الْخُدْرِيِّ، أَنَّ رَسُولَ اللهِ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ قَالَ: «لَا ضَرَرَ وَلَا ضِرَارَ، مَنْ ضَارَّ ضَرَّهُ اللَّهُ، وَمَنْ شَاقَّ شَقَّ اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ» (سنن الدارقطني، بَابُ كِتَابِ الْبُيُوعِ، رقم 3079)
وَجْهُ (3): الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ ذَلِكَ: قَالَ: قَالَ لِي الْعَدَّاءُ بْنُ خَالِدِ بْنِ هَوْذَةَ: أَلَا أُقْرِئُكَ كِتَابًا كَتَبَهُ لِي رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ؟ قَالَ: قُلْتُ: بَلَى، فَأَخْرَجَ لِي كِتَابًا: «هَذَا مَا اشْتَرَى الْعَدَّاءُ بْنُ خَالِدِ بْنِ هَوْذَةَ مِنْ مُحَمَّدٍ رَسُولِ اللَّهِ، اشْتَرَى مِنْهُ عَبْدًا أَوْ أَمَةً، لَا دَاءَ وَلَا غَائِلَةَ وَلَا خِبْثَةَ، بَيْعَ الْمُسْلِمِ الْمُسْلِمَ» (سنن الترمذي، بَابُ مَا جَاءَ فِي كِتَابَةِ الشُّرُوطِ، رقم 1216)
وَجْهُ (4): الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ ذَلِكَ: عَنْ أَنَسِ بْنِ مَالِكٍ، أَنَّ رَسُولَ اللهِ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ بَاعَ حِلْسًا وَقَدَحًا وَقَالَ: «مَنْ يَشْتَرِي هَذَا الْحِلْسَ وَالْقَدَحَ»، فَقَالَ رَجُلٌ: أَخَذْتُهُمَا بِدِرْهَمٍ، فَقَالَ النَّبِيُّ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ: «مَنْ يَزِيدُ عَلَى دِرْهَمٍ؟ مَنْ يَزِيدُ عَلَى دِرْهَمٍ؟»، فَأَعْطَاهُ رَجُلٌ دِرْهَمَيْنِ، فَبَاعَهُمَا مِنْهُ (سنن الترمذي، بَابُ مَا جَاءَ فِي بَيْعِ مَنْ يَزِيدُ، رقم 1218)
وَجْهُ (5): الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ ذَلِكَ: عَنِ ابْنِ عُمَرَ رَضِيَ اللهُ عَنْهُمَا قَالَ: كُنَّا مَعَ النَّبِيِّ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ فِي سَفَرٍ، فَكُنْتُ عَلَى بَكْرٍ صَعْبٍ لِعُمَرَ... فَقَالَ النَّبِيُّ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ لِعُمَرَ: «بِعْنِيهِ»، قَالَ: هُوَ لَكَ يَا رَسُولَ اللَّهِ، قَالَ: «بِعْنِيهِ»، فَبَاعَهُ مِنْ رَسُولِ اللَّهِ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ، فَقَالَ النَّبِيُّ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ: «هُوَ لَكَ يَا عَبْدَ اللَّهِ بْنَ عُمَرَ، تَصْنَعُ بِهِ مَا شِئْتَ» (بخاري: بَابٌ: إِذَا اشْتَرَى شَيْئًا فَوَهَبَ مِنْ سَاعَتِهِ قَبْلَ أَنْ يَتَفَرَّقَا، رقم 2115)
أُصُولٌ: بَيْعُ الشِّرَاءِ إِيجَابٌ وَقَبُولٌ ضَرُورِيٌّ، لِأَنَّ بِهِ تَظْهَرُ رِضَا الْبَائِعِ وَالْمُشْتَرِي.
Book of Sales. He (the author) said - may Allah have mercy on him -: The sale is contracted by offer and acceptance when they are in the past tense. First evidence: the verse to establish that: 'And Allah has permitted sale and forbidden usury' (Surah al-Baqarah, 257). Second evidence: the hadith to establish that: from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'No harm and no mutual harm; whoever causes harm, Allah will harm him, and whoever makes things difficult, Allah will make things difficult for him' (Sunan al-Daraqutni, chapter of book of sales, no. 3079). Third evidence: the hadith to establish that: He said: 'Al-Adda' ibn Khalid ibn Hawdhah said to me: Shall I recite to you a letter that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) wrote for me?' He said: I said yes, so he brought out a letter: 'This is what al-Adda' ibn Khalid ibn Hawdhah bought from Muhammad, the Messenger of Allah; he bought from him a male or female slave, with no disease, no deceit, no defect, a sale of a Muslim to a Muslim' (Sunan al-Tirmidhi, chapter of what came concerning writing conditions, no. 1216). Fourth evidence: the hadith to establish that: from Anas ibn Malik that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) sold a saddlecloth and a cup, and said: 'Who will buy this saddlecloth and cup?' A man said: 'I take them for a dirham.' The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Who will give more than a dirham? Who will give more than a dirham?' So a man gave two dirhams, and he sold them to him (Sunan al-Tirmidhi, chapter of what came concerning sale by bidding, no. 1218). Fifth evidence: the hadith to establish that: from Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with them both) said: We were with the Prophet (peace be upon him) on a journey, and I was on a difficult camel of Umar... The Prophet (peace be upon him) said to Umar: 'Sell it to me.' He said: 'It is for you, O Messenger of Allah.' He said: 'Sell it to me.' So he sold it to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him). The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'It is for you, O Abdullah ibn Umar; do with it as you wish' (Bukhari: chapter: if one buys something and gives it as a gift before parting, no. 2115). Principle: The sale of purchase requires offer and acceptance necessarily, because through them the consent of seller and buyer becomes apparent.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Sales in Al-Quduri establishes a fundamental rule of Hanafi contract law: a sale contract is concluded (yan'aqidu) only through offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) expressed in the past tense (lafz al-madi). The past tense indicates immediate and definitive consent, as opposed to future tense which implies a promise. The author then provides multiple proofs: first, the Quranic verse that permits sale and forbids riba, implying that sale requires structure. Then five hadiths, including the prohibition of harm (indicating that valid contracts prevent harm), the written contract of al-Adda' (using 'ishtara' in past tense), the auction of the Prophet (where he used 'bā'a' past tense), and the sale of the camel to Ibn Umar (using 'bi'ni' which is a command but the response was past tense). The usul note emphasizes that ijab and qabul are necessary to manifest the consent (rida) of both parties. Thus, the Hanafi position is clear: a sale without past-tense expression is invalid.

Key Terms
البيع (al-bay'): A contract of exchange of property with mutual consent, typically involving transfer of ownership.
الإيجاب والقبول (al-ijab wa al-qabul): The offer and acceptance that form the contract; in sales, both must be expressed in the past tense.
لفظ الماضي (lafz al-madi): The past tense form of a verb, e.g., 'ba'tu' (I sold), 'ishtaraytu' (I bought).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, a sale contract is contracted by offer and acceptance in which tense?
A) Present tense
B) Past tense
C) Future tense
D) Any tense is acceptable

Key Takeaway
Offer and acceptance in past tense are essential for a valid sale in Hanafi law; without it, no binding contract exists.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner
6 min
Sale Contract Concluded by Offer and Acceptance in Past Tense
States the first ruling of Kitab al-Buyu' with five textual evidences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the formation of a sale contract and identify the key evidence from Quran and Sunnah that supports the requirement of past tense.

Core Idea
A sale contract is formed (yan'qid) only when both offer and acceptance are expressed in the past tense – the tense of immediate commitment.

This lesson opens the Book of Sales (Kitab al-Buyu') with the foundational ruling that a sale contract is concluded by offer and acceptance in the past tense. The author then provides five pieces of evidence from the Quran and Sunnah to support this ruling. Understanding this ruling is essential for every Muslim engaged in trade, as it defines the moment ownership is transferred.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? (Sale contract) What is the ruling? (It is concluded by offer and acceptance in past tense.)
The ruling establishes validity (sahih) when the condition is met.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
Read the verse 'And Allah has permitted trade...' How does general permission imply the validity of the contract form?
The verse is general, so any mutual agreement in sales is allowed unless specifically prohibited.
3
Examine the hadith evidence
Review each of the four hadiths: harm prohibition, written contract, auction-like sale, and the camel purchase. How does each support the past tense requirement?
The hadiths show the Prophet using and accepting past tense in actual transactions.
4
Understand the principle behind past tense
Why does the Hanafi school insist on past tense? What does it indicate about commitment?
Past tense indicates immediate, definitive commitment, whereas future tense implies a promise or condition.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Given a scenario where a seller says 'I will sell you this for $10' and buyer says 'I agree,' determine the validity.
The contract is not valid because the offer is in future tense, not past.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كِتَابُ الْبُيُوع
قَالَ - رَحِمَهُ الله - الْبَيْعُ يَنْعَقِدُ بِالإِيجَابِ وَالْقَبُولِ إِذَا كَانَا بِلَفْظِ الْمَاضِي

وجه : (1) الآية: وَأَحَلَّ اللَّهُ الْبَيْعَ وَحَرَّمَ الرِّبَا (سورة البقرة، 2 آيت نمبر 257)
وجه : (۲) الحديث: عَنْ أَبِي سَعِيدٍ الخُدْرِي، أَنَّ رَسُولَ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «لَا ضَرَرَ وَلَا صِرَارَ...» (سنن دار قطني)
وجه: (1) الحديث: قَالَ: قَالَ لِي العَدَّاءُ بْنُ خَالِدِ بْنِ هَوْذَةَ: أَلَا أُقْرِئُكَ كِتَابًا كَتَبَهُ لِي رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم... «هَذَا مَا اشْتَرَى العَدَّاءُ بْنُ خَالِدِ بْن هَوْذَةَ مِنْ مُحَمَّدٍ رَسُولِ اللَّهِ ...» (سنن الترمذي)
وجه (۲) الحديث: عَنْ أَنَسِ بْنِ مَالِكٍ، أَنَّ رَسُولَ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم بَاعَ حِلْسًا وَقَدَحًا وَقَالَ: «مَنْ يَشْتَرِي هَذَا الحِلْسَ وَالْقَدَحَ»، فَقَالَ رَجُلٌ: أَخَذْتُهُمَا بِدِرْهَم، فَقَالَ النَّبِيُّ الله : «مَنْ يَزيدُ عَلَى دِرْهَم ...» (سنن الترمذي)
وجه (۳) الحديث: عَنِ ابْنِ عُمَرَ الله عنهما قَالَ: كُنَّا مَعَ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم فِي سَفَرٍ، فَكُنْتُ عَلَى بَكْرٍ صَعْبٍ لِعُمَرَ ... فَقَالَ النَّبِيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم لِعُمَرَ : بِعْنِيهِ، قَالَ : هُوَ لَكَ يَا رَسُولَ اللَّهِ، قَالَ: بِعْنِيهِ، فَبَاعَهُ مِنْ رَسُولِ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم فَقَالَ النَّبِيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم: هُوَ لَكَ يَا عَبْدَ اللَّهِ بْنَ عُمَرَ، تَصْنَعُ بِهِ مَا شِئْتَ» (بخاري)

اصول : بیچ دشر اء میں ایجاب و قبول ضروری ہے، کیونکہ اس کے ذریعے بائع ومشتری کی رضا ظاہر ہوتی ہے۔
The book of sales. He (the author) said - may Allah have mercy on him - Sale is concluded by offer and acceptance when both are in the past tense. Evidence: (1) The verse: 'And Allah has permitted trade and forbidden interest' (Surah al-Baqarah, 2:275). (2) The hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: 'No harm and no reciprocating harm...' (Sunan al-Daraqutni). (3) The hadith of al-'Adda' ibn Khalid: 'This is what al-'Adda' ibn Khalid ibn Hawdhah bought from Muhammad the Messenger of Allah...' (Sunan al-Tirmidhi). (4) The hadith of Anas ibn Malik: 'The Prophet sold a saddle blanket and a cup...' (Sunan al-Tirmidhi). (5) The hadith of Ibn 'Umar: 'We were with the Prophet on a journey...' (Bukhari). Principle: In a sale, offer and acceptance are necessary because through them the consent of the seller and buyer is manifest.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Sales (Kitab al-Buyu') by stating the foundational ruling that a sale contract is concluded (yan'qid) by means of offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) expressed in the past tense. The Hanafi position is that the past tense is the standard form for contractual expressions, as it indicates immediate and definitive commitment. The author then provides five pieces of evidence: first, the Quranic verse 'Allah has permitted trade' which implies the validity of contracts through mutual consent; second, the hadith prohibiting harm, which underpins the requirement of explicit consent; third, a written contract from the Prophet himself using past tense; fourth, the Prophet's example of selling by asking 'Who will buy?' and the reply 'I take them'; fifth, the well-known hadith of Ibn Umar where the Prophet bought a camel using past tense. The Urdu note clarifies that the purpose of offer and acceptance is to manifest the mutual satisfaction (rida) of the parties.

Key Terms
إيجاب (ijab): In this text, ijab refers to the first statement made by one party indicating willingness to sell or buy, typically in past tense.
قبول (qabul): The second statement made by the other party in response to the ijab, accepting the offer in past tense.
بيع (bay'): The contract of sale, defined as the exchange of property for property with mutual consent.
لفظ الماضي (lafz al-madi): The past tense form used in the offer and acceptance, indicating immediate execution of the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a valid form of offer in a sale contract according to the Hanafi school?
A) 'I sell you this for 10 dirhams' in past tense
B) 'I will sell you this for 10 dirhams' in future tense
C) 'Take this for 10 dirhams' in past tense
D) 'I have sold you this for 10 dirhams' in past tense

Key Takeaway
A sale contract is concluded only when offer and acceptance are expressed in the past tense, as established by Quranic permission and Prophetic practice.

Khiyar al-Majlis: The Option of the Council in Sales
Explains the rule of khiyar al-majlis and provides four proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-majlis, explain when the sale becomes binding, and identify the key proofs from the Sunnah and sayings of the Companions.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, both parties retain the option to cancel the sale until they physically separate.

This lesson presents the foundational ruling on the option of the council (khiyar al-majlis) in sales, a distinctive feature of the Hanafi school. You will learn how the sale becomes binding only after offer and acceptance are completed in the same session, and how the option ends with physical separation. The text also provides four proofs, including a well-known hadith and sayings of the Companions, to reinforce the ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the central ruling of khiyar al-majlis
Identify the rule: when one party makes an offer, the other has the option to accept or reject in the same session. If the offeree leaves without accepting, the offer becomes void. Once offer and acceptance are exchanged, the sale is binding and no further option remains except for hidden defects or lack of sight.
2
Identify the conditions for the option and its termination
The option exists only as long as both parties remain in the same physical session. Separation (standing up and leaving) before acceptance voids the offer. After acceptance, the sale is binding; only exceptions like hidden defect allow rescission.
Note that the exception for hidden defect is a separate right, not a continuation of khiyar al-majlis.
3
Examine the primary proof (hadith)
The hadith of Hakim ibn Hizam: 'The two parties to a sale have the option as long as they have not separated.' This establishes the option until physical separation. The phrase 'ما لم يتفرقا' is the key linguistic indicator.
The hadith is narrated in Bukhari, Muslim, and Abu Dawood.
4
Review the supplementary proofs (athar)
The passage quotes: (2) The saying of Umar: 'The sale is either by agreement or by option, and every Muslim has his condition.' (3) The saying of Sufyan: 'The handshake is with the tongue' emphasizing verbal conclusion. (4) Another hadith from Abu Hurairah with similar wording.
These strengthen the Hanafi position that the option exists until separation and that the contract is verbal.
5
Recognize the cross-madhhab difference
The Hanafi school (along with Shafi'i) recognizes khiyar al-majlis, while the Maliki and Hanbali schools do not in the same form. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on differing interpretations of the hadith and usul.
Understanding this difference helps in comparative fiqh and real-world application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا أَوْجَبَ أَحَدُ الْمُتَعَاقِدَيْنِ الْبَيْعَ فَالْآخَرُ بِالْخِيَارِ إِنْ شَاءَ قَبِلَهُ فِي الْمَجْلِسِ وَإِنْ شَاءَ رَدَّهُ وَأَيُّهُمَا قَامَ مِنْ الْمَجْلِسِ قَبْلَ الْقَبُولِ بَطَلَ الْإِيجَابُ
فَإِذَا حَصَلَ الْإِيجَابُ وَالْقَبُولُ لَزِمَ الْبَيْعُ وَلا خِيَارَ لِوَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا إِلَّا مِنْ غَيْبِ أَوْ عَدَمِ رُؤْيَةٍ

وجه : (۱) الحديث: حَكِيمِ بْنِ حِزَامٍ هُ قَالَ: قَالَ رَسُولُ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «الْبَيْعَانِ بِالْخِيَارِ مَا لَمْ يَتَفَرَّقَا » (بخاري / مسلم / ابوداود)
وجه : (۲) قول الصحابي: وَقَالَ عُمَرُ، الْبَيْعُ عَنْ صَفْقَةٍ، أَوْ خِيَارٍ، وَلَكُلِّ مُسْلِمٍ شَرْطُهُ» (مصنف عبد الرزاق)
وجه (۳) قول الصحابي: قَالَ سُفْيَانُ: «وَالصَّفْقَةُ بِاللَّسَانِ» (مصنف عبد الرزاق)
وجه : (۴) الحديث: عَنْ أَبِي هُرَيْرَةَ، قَالَ: قَالَ رَسُولُ الله : «الْبَيْعَانِ بِالْخِيَارِ مَا لَمْ يَفْتَرِقَا مِنْ بَيْعِهِمَا ، أَوْ يَكُونُ بَيْعُهُمَا بِخِيَارٍ» (مصنف ابن ابي شيبه)
And when one of the two contracting parties makes an offer, the other has the option: if he wishes he may accept it in the session, and if he wishes he may reject it. And whichever of them leaves the session before acceptance, the offer becomes void. So when offer and acceptance occur, the sale becomes binding, and neither of them has any option except due to a hidden defect or lack of sight. Evidence: (1) The hadith of Hakim ibn Hizam: the Messenger of Allah said: 'The two parties to a sale have the option as long as they have not separated.' (2) The saying of Umar: 'The sale is either by agreement or by option, and every Muslim has his condition.' (3) The saying of Sufyan: 'The handshake is with the tongue.' (4) The hadith of Abu Hurairah: the Messenger of Allah said: 'The two parties to a sale have the option as long as they have not separated from their sale, or their sale is with an option.'
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage states the ruling for khiyar al-majlis (option of the council), which is a distinctive feature of the Hanafi school. The offeror's statement creates a right but not an immediate obligation; the offeree may accept or reject during the same sitting. If the offeree leaves without accepting, the offer lapses. Once both parties exchange offer and acceptance in past tense, the sale is binding and no further option remains except in cases of hidden defect (ghayb) or non-vision (adam ru'ya) of the object. The author provides four evidences: the well-known hadith 'The two parties to a sale have the option as long as they have not separated'; the saying of Umar that a sale is either concluded by consensus or with an option; the saying of Sufyan that 'the handshake is with the tongue' (meaning the verbal exchange is what matters); and another hadith from Abu Hurairah. These support the Hanafi ruling that the option is tied to the physical session and ends with separation.

Key Terms
خيار المجلس (khiyar al-majlis): The option available to each party to a sale contract to cancel or confirm the sale as long as they remain in the same sitting.
مجلس (majlis): The physical meeting place where the contracting parties are present and the negotiation takes place.
خيار (khiyar): The right to cancel or continue with the sale; a conditional option.
صفقة (safqah): The concluding statement or handshake that confirms the sale. In the saying of Umar, it refers to the final agreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what terminates the khiyar al-majlis?
A) The passing of three days
B) Physical separation of the parties
C) The seller's acceptance of the offer
D) The buyer's inspection of the goods

Key Takeaway
The khiyar al-majlis is a temporary option that ends when the parties physically separate or when they complete the contract; after that, the sale is binding except for hidden defects.

Sale of Pointed-to Items: Quantity Not Required
States a legal ruling with two hadith proofs and a usul distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling present, pointed-to items without specifying quantity and identify the supporting hadith evidence.

Core Idea
A present item pointed to needs no quantity measure — sight suffices to cure uncertainty.

This lesson examines a core Hanafi principle in sales: when the object of sale is physically present and pointed to, the parties are not required to specify its exact quantity. This ruling is based on the Prophetic practice of juzaf (buying by estimation) and the general principle of mutual consent. Understanding this simplifies everyday transactions and distinguishes the Hanafi approach from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Locate the legal statement: 'The considerations that are pointed to do not require knowledge of their quantity for the permissibility of the sale.' Note that this applies only when the item is present.
The Arabic 'a'wad' means the objects exchanged (goods and price).
2
Examine the Dalil (Evidence)
Two hadith are cited. First: 'The two should not separate except by mutual consent' – this implies that if both agree after seeing the item, the contract is valid. Second: the report of Ibn Umar that people bought food by estimation (juzaf) in the Prophet's time.
Juzaf means buying by sight/approximation, without exact measurement.
3
Understand the Usul Distinction
The Urdu note clarifies the principle: if the commodity is absent (gha'ib), quantity and description are required; if present and pointed to, pointing is sufficient.
This distinction is a key Hanafi rule: sight removes the uncertainty (gharar) that would otherwise invalidate the sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْأَعْوَاضُ الْمُشَارُ إِلَيْهَا لَا يَحْتَاجُ إلَى مَعْرِفَةِ مِقْدَارِهَا فِي جَوَازِ الْبَيْعِ

وجه: (1) الحديث: سَمِعْتُ أَبَا هُرَيْرَةَ، يَقُولُ: قَالَ رَسُولُ الله : لَا يَفْتَرَقَنَّ اثْنَانِ إِلَّا عَنْ تَرَاضِ» (ابوداود)
وجه (۲) الحديث: أَنَّ ابْنَ عُمَرَ رضي الله عنهما قَالَ: «لَقَدْ رَأَيْتُ النَّاسَ فِي عَهْدِ رَسُولِ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم يَبْتَاعُونَ جُزَافًا، يَعْنِي الطَّعَامَ »(بخاري / مسلم)

اصول : طبیج غائب ہو تو مقدار اور صفت بیان کرنا ضروری ہے لیکن طبیع موجود اور مشار ہو تو اشارہ کافی ہے ۔
And the considerations that are pointed to do not require knowledge of their quantity for the permissibility of the sale. Evidence: (1) The hadith: I heard Abu Hurairah say: The Messenger of Allah said: 'Two should not separate except by mutual consent.' (2) The hadith: Ibn Umar said: 'I saw people in the time of the Messenger of Allah buying by estimation (juzafan), meaning food.' Principle: If the commodity is absent, it is necessary to state its quantity and description, but if it is present and pointed to, pointing is sufficient.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage addresses a practical scenario: when the objects of exchange (a'wad, referring to the price or the commodity being sold) are physically present and can be pointed to, the parties need not specify the exact measurement or count. This is based on the principle that sight of the object conveys sufficient knowledge to avoid future disputes. The author provides two hadith evidences: first, the command not to separate except by mutual consent implies that if both parties agree after seeing the item, it is valid even without precise quantity; second, the practice of buying food by estimation (juzaf) in the Prophet's time shows that approximate quantity without precise measurement is acceptable. The Urdu note clarifies the distinction: if the commodity is absent (gha'ib), its quantity and description must be stated to avoid uncertainty; but if it is present and pointed to (mushar), mere indication is sufficient. This ruling is specific to the Hanafi school and simplifies transactions for present goods.

Key Terms
أعراض (a'wad): In this context, a'wad refers to the objects of exchange in a sale, i.e., the goods and the price.
مشار إليه (mushar ilayh): An item that is physically present and pointed to during the contract, removing the need for detailed description.
جزاف (juzaf): Buying or selling by estimation without exact measurement, based on sight or approximation.
غائب (gha'ib): An item not physically present at the time of contract; requires description of quantity and quality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is it permissible to sell an item without specifying its exact quantity?
A) Always, regardless of whether the item is present or absent
B) Only when the item is present and can be pointed to
C) Only when the item is absent and described in detail
D) Never; quantity must always be specified

Key Takeaway
For present, pointed-to items, the Hanafi school dispenses with quantity specification, relying on sight to cure uncertainty, as evidenced by the Prophetic practice of juzaf.

Conditions of Price: Known Amount, Quality, and Term
Quduri ruling on price conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for a valid price in a Hanafi sale and identify when a sale becomes fasid due to price ambiguity.

Core Idea
A sale's price must be known in amount and quality, with a known term if deferred, or the sale is fasid.

In Hanafi fiqh, the price (thaman) is a critical element in any sale. The Quduri matn opens with a clear rule: the price must be known in quantity and quality. This lesson unpacks that rule, including the permission for deferred sales with a known term and the requirement to specify the currency when multiple currencies coexist. These rulings are supported by the hadith of Ibn Abbas on salam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is being ruled upon? The price (thaman) in a sale. The ruling: the price must be known in quantity and quality; if deferred, the term must be known; if multiple currencies, one must be specified. The consequence of ambiguity is fasid (corrupt).
This is the foundational condition for a valid sale.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith of Ibn Abbas on salam: 'Whoever pays in advance for something, let it be with a known measure, a known weight, and a known term.' This hadith is cited as evidence that the price and term must be known.
Sunnah ahad, sahih.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The command to specify measure and weight is extended to the price in a regular sale because the same principle of avoiding gharar applies. The term must also be known for deferred sales.
The hadith directly addresses salam, but by analogy (qiyas) or by direct implication, the ruling applies to all sales.
4
Apply the rule to currency
If the price is left unspecified as to currency type, it defaults to the prevalent coin of the town. If currencies are mixed and unspecified, the sale is fasid because the actual price is unknown.
This prevents ambiguity when multiple currencies circulate.
5
Identify the consequence of violation
If any of these conditions (known amount, known quality, known term if deferred, known currency if mixed) are missing, the sale is fasid, meaning it is corrupt and must be rectified or cancelled.
Fasid is distinct from batil (void); fasid can be corrected if the parties agree to a valid price.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْأَثْمَانُ الْمُطْلَقَةُ لَا تَصِحُ إِلَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ مَعْرُوفَةَ الْقَدْرِ وَالصَّفَةِ وَيَجُوزُ الْبَيْعُ بِثَمَنٍ حَالٍ أَوْ مُؤَجَّلٍ إِذَا كَانَ الْأَجَلُ مَعْلُومًا وَمَنْ أَطْلَقَ الثَّمَنَ فِي الْبَيْعِ كَانَ عَلَى غَالِبِ نَقْدِ الْبَلَدِ فَإِنْ كَانَتْ النُّقُودُ مُخْتَلِفَةً فَالْبَيْعُ فَاسِدٌ إِلَّا أَنْ يُبَيِّنَ أَحَدَهَا وَيَجُوزُ بَيْعُ الطَّعَامِ وَالْحْبُوبِ كُلِّهَا مُكَايَلَةً وَمُجَازَفَةً وَبِإِنَاءٍ بِعَيْنِهِ لَا يُعْرَفُ مِقْدَارُهُ وَبِوَزْنِ حَجَرٍ لَا يُعْرَفُ مِقْدَارُهُ وَمَنْ بَاعَ صُبْرَةَ طَعَامٍ كُلَّ قَفِيزِ بِدِرْهَم جَازَ الْبَيْعُ فِي قَفِيزٍ وَاحِدٍ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ إِلَّا أَنْ يُسَمِّيَ جُمْلَةَ قُفْزَانِهَا وَمَنْ بَاعَ قَطِيعَ غَنَمٍ كُلَّ شَاةٍ بِدِرْهَم فَالْبَيْعُ فَاسِدٌ فِي جَمِيعِهَا وَكَذَلِكَ مَنْ بَاعَ ثَوْبًا مُذَارَعَةً كُلَّ ذِرَاعٍ بِدِرْهَم وَلَمْ يُسَمِّ جُمْلَةَ الذِّرْعَانِ وَمَنْ بَاعَ صُبْرَةَ طَعَامٍ عَلَى أَنَّهَا مِائَةُ قَفِيزٍ بِمِائَةِ دِرْهَم فَوَجَدَهَا أَقَلَّ فَالْمُشْتَرِي بِالْخِيَارِ إِنْ شَاءَ أَخَذَ الْمَوْجُودَ بِحِصَّتِهِ وَإِنْ شَاءَ فَسَخَ الْبَيْعَ وَإِنْ وَجَدَهَا أَكْثَرَ مِنْ ذَلِكَ فَالزِّيَادَةُ لِلْبَائِعِ
Absolute prices are not valid unless they are known in amount and quality. Sale with an immediate or deferred price is valid if the term is known. Whoever leaves the price unspecified in a sale, it is based on the common currency of the town. If the currencies are mixed, the sale is invalid unless he specifies one of them. The sale of all food and grains is valid by measure, by estimation, by a specific container whose capacity is unknown, and by a stone weight whose weight is unknown. Whoever sells a heap of grain, each qafiz for a dirham, the sale is valid for one qafiz according to Abu Hanifa, unless he specifies the total number of its qafizes. Whoever sells a flock of sheep, each sheep for a dirham, the sale is invalid for all of them. Similarly, whoever sells a cloth by the cubit, each cubit for a dirham, and does not specify the total number of cubits. Whoever sells a heap of grain on the basis that it is one hundred qafiz for one hundred dirhams, and finds it to be less, then the buyer has the option: if he wishes, he takes the existing amount at its proportionate price, and if he wishes, he cancels the sale. If he finds it to be more, the excess belongs to the seller.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage from Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar presents key rulings on the conditions of price (thaman) and the validity of various sale contracts, as elaborated by the Hanafi school. The first ruling states that the price must be known in both quantity and quality; otherwise, the sale is invalid due to gharar. This is supported by hadith prohibiting gharar and the hadith of Ibn Abbas on salam requiring known measure, weight, and term. The second ruling allows both spot and deferred sales provided the term is known, citing the Quranic verse 'Allah has permitted trade' and the practice of the Prophet purchasing food on credit. The third addresses unspecified currency: if a buyer does not specify the type of money, it defaults to the prevalent coinage of the region; if multiple currencies coexist without specification, the sale is corrupt (fasid) because the subject matter is ambiguous. The fourth ruling permits selling food and grains by measure or even by estimation (juzaf), as well as by unknown containers or stones, reflecting the Hanafi leniency to facilitate trade, supported by the practice of the Companions buying food in bulk without exact measurement. The subsequent rulings deal with per-unit sales without specifying the total quantity: Abu Hanifa holds that selling a heap of grain at a per-unit price (e.g., each qafiz for a dirham) validates the sale only for one unit unless the total is stated, because the individual units cannot be distinguished. However, for non-fungible items like sheep or cloth, such a sale is entirely invalid (fasid) because the per-unit pricing cannot be applied without total specification. Finally, if a heap is sold as a specific total (e.g., 100 qafiz for 100 dirhams) and turns out to be less, the buyer may either take the actual amount at a proportionate price or cancel; if more, the excess belongs to the seller, as per a narration from Ikrimah. This entire section illustrates the Hanafi emphasis on removing ambiguity (gharar) in sales while allowing flexibility in common transactions.

Key Terms
ثَمَن (thaman): Price or consideration in a sale; must be known in quantity and quality to avoid gharar.
مُجَازَفَة (mujazafah): Sale by estimation without exact measurement; valid for food and grains according to Hanafis.
قَفِيز (qafiz): A unit of dry measure; used for grain in sales.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a city where both dirhams and dinars circulate, a buyer agrees to pay 'one thousand' without specifying which currency. The sale is:
A) Valid, based on the common currency of the town.
B) Fasid, because the currency is not specified when multiple currencies coexist.
C) Valid if the buyer pays in the currency the seller expects.
D) Batil and cannot be rectified.

Key Takeaway
The price must be known in amount and quality; the term must be known for deferred sales; and in case of mixed currency, the currency must be specified, or the sale is fasid.

The Permissibility of Selling Food and Grains by Estimation (Juzaf)
Quduri ruling on juzaf sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that food and grains may be sold by estimation (juzaf) and explain the rationale based on facilitation of trade.

Core Idea
Hanafi school permits selling food by estimation, even without exact measure, to ease transactions.

After addressing price conditions, the Quduri matn turns to a practical ruling: food and grains can be sold by measure, by estimation (juzaf), or using containers of unknown capacity. This reflects the Hanafi leniency to facilitate trade, as opposed to other schools that require exact measurement to avoid gharar. This lesson explores that ruling and the underlying reason based on istihsan and the practice of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and permitted methods
The passage rules that the sale of all food and grains is valid by measure (by exact quantity), by estimation (juzaf), by a specific container whose capacity is unknown, and by a stone weight whose weight is unknown. Note that these methods involve some uncertainty in quantity.
The ruling applies to 'all food and grains' – a broad category.
2
Recognize the absence of a direct hadith in the text
Unlike the previous ruling on price conditions, the matn does not cite a hadith for juzaf. The basis is istihsan (juristic preference) and the practice of the Companions (Urf) who commonly bought and sold food without exact measurement.
Istihsan is a key Hanafi principle that allows departure from qiyas for a stronger reason.
3
Note the cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i and Maliki schools require exact measurement for such sales to avoid gharar. Hanafis argue that estimation is common practice and requiring exact measurement would cause hardship.
This is a legitimate difference in ijtihad.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
If a seller offers a pile of wheat for 500 dirhams by estimation (without weighing), that sale is valid in the Hanafi school. The buyer trusts the seller's assessment, and the sale is complete.
If the buyer later discovers a significant discrepancy, he may have a claim, but the initial sale is not invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْأَثْمَانُ الْمُطْلَقَةُ لَا تَصِحُ إِلَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ مَعْرُوفَةَ الْقَدْرِ وَالصَّفَةِ وَيَجُوزُ الْبَيْعُ بِثَمَنٍ حَالٍ أَوْ مُؤَجَّلٍ إِذَا كَانَ الْأَجَلُ مَعْلُومًا وَمَنْ أَطْلَقَ الثَّمَنَ فِي الْبَيْعِ كَانَ عَلَى غَالِبِ نَقْدِ الْبَلَدِ فَإِنْ كَانَتْ النُّقُودُ مُخْتَلِفَةً فَالْبَيْعُ فَاسِدٌ إِلَّا أَنْ يُبَيِّنَ أَحَدَهَا وَيَجُوزُ بَيْعُ الطَّعَامِ وَالْحْبُوبِ كُلِّهَا مُكَايَلَةً وَمُجَازَفَةً وَبِإِنَاءٍ بِعَيْنِهِ لَا يُعْرَفُ مِقْدَارُهُ وَبِوَزْنِ حَجَرٍ لَا يُعْرَفُ مِقْدَارُهُ وَمَنْ بَاعَ صُبْرَةَ طَعَامٍ كُلَّ قَفِيزِ بِدِرْهَم جَازَ الْبَيْعُ فِي قَفِيزٍ وَاحِدٍ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ إِلَّا أَنْ يُسَمِّيَ جُمْلَةَ قُفْزَانِهَا وَمَنْ بَاعَ قَطِيعَ غَنَمٍ كُلَّ شَاةٍ بِدِرْهَم فَالْبَيْعُ فَاسِدٌ فِي جَمِيعِهَا وَكَذَلِكَ مَنْ بَاعَ ثَوْبًا مُذَارَعَةً كُلَّ ذِرَاعٍ بِدِرْهَم وَلَمْ يُسَمِّ جُمْلَةَ الذِّرْعَانِ وَمَنْ بَاعَ صُبْرَةَ طَعَامٍ عَلَى أَنَّهَا مِائَةُ قَفِيزٍ بِمِائَةِ دِرْهَم فَوَجَدَهَا أَقَلَّ فَالْمُشْتَرِي بِالْخِيَارِ إِنْ شَاءَ أَخَذَ الْمَوْجُودَ بِحِصَّتِهِ وَإِنْ شَاءَ فَسَخَ الْبَيْعَ وَإِنْ وَجَدَهَا أَكْثَرَ مِنْ ذَلِكَ فَالزِّيَادَةُ لِلْبَائِعِ
Absolute prices are not valid unless they are known in amount and quality. Sale with an immediate or deferred price is valid if the term is known. Whoever leaves the price unspecified in a sale, it is based on the common currency of the town. If the currencies are mixed, the sale is invalid unless he specifies one of them. The sale of all food and grains is valid by measure, by estimation, by a specific container whose capacity is unknown, and by a stone weight whose weight is unknown. Whoever sells a heap of grain, each qafiz for a dirham, the sale is valid for one qafiz according to Abu Hanifa, unless he specifies the total number of its qafizes. Whoever sells a flock of sheep, each sheep for a dirham, the sale is invalid for all of them. Similarly, whoever sells a cloth by the cubit, each cubit for a dirham, and does not specify the total number of cubits. Whoever sells a heap of grain on the basis that it is one hundred qafiz for one hundred dirhams, and finds it to be less, then the buyer has the option: if he wishes, he takes the existing amount at its proportionate price, and if he wishes, he cancels the sale. If he finds it to be more, the excess belongs to the seller.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage from Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar presents key rulings on the conditions of price (thaman) and the validity of various sale contracts, as elaborated by the Hanafi school. The first ruling states that the price must be known in both quantity and quality; otherwise, the sale is invalid due to gharar. This is supported by hadith prohibiting gharar and the hadith of Ibn Abbas on salam requiring known measure, weight, and term. The second ruling allows both spot and deferred sales provided the term is known, citing the Quranic verse 'Allah has permitted trade' and the practice of the Prophet purchasing food on credit. The third addresses unspecified currency: if a buyer does not specify the type of money, it defaults to the prevalent coinage of the region; if multiple currencies coexist without specification, the sale is corrupt (fasid) because the subject matter is ambiguous. The fourth ruling permits selling food and grains by measure or even by estimation (juzaf), as well as by unknown containers or stones, reflecting the Hanafi leniency to facilitate trade, supported by the practice of the Companions buying food in bulk without exact measurement. The subsequent rulings deal with per-unit sales without specifying the total quantity: Abu Hanifa holds that selling a heap of grain at a per-unit price (e.g., each qafiz for a dirham) validates the sale only for one unit unless the total is stated, because the individual units cannot be distinguished. However, for non-fungible items like sheep or cloth, such a sale is entirely invalid (fasid) because the per-unit pricing cannot be applied without total specification. Finally, if a heap is sold as a specific total (e.g., 100 qafiz for 100 dirhams) and turns out to be less, the buyer may either take the actual amount at a proportionate price or cancel; if more, the excess belongs to the seller, as per a narration from Ikrimah. This entire section illustrates the Hanafi emphasis on removing ambiguity (gharar) in sales while allowing flexibility in common transactions.

Key Terms
ثَمَن (thaman): Price or consideration in a sale; must be known in quantity and quality to avoid gharar.
مُجَازَفَة (mujazafah): Sale by estimation without exact measurement; valid for food and grains according to Hanafis.
قَفِيز (qafiz): A unit of dry measure; used for grain in sales.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, a sale of a heap of grain by estimation (juzaf) is:
A) Invalid due to gharar.
B) Valid only if the seller specifies the total quantity.
C) Valid as an exception based on istihsan.
D) Valid only for containers of known capacity.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi school permits selling food and grains by estimation (juzaf), by unknown containers, and by stones of unknown weight, as an exception to the rule against gharar to facilitate trade.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Per-Unit Sales: The Distinction Between Fungibles and Non-Fungibles
Quduri rulings on per-unit sales and quantity discrepancy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that selling a fungible heap per unit is valid for one unit, while selling non-fungibles per unit without total is invalid, and state the ruling on shortfall or excess in a lump sum sale.

Core Idea
Selling grain per qafiz is valid for one qafiz; selling sheep per head or cloth per cubit without total is wholly invalid.

The Quduri matn presents a nuanced ruling: selling a heap of grain at a per-unit price (each qafiz for a dirham) is valid for one unit only, according to Abu Hanifa, unless the total is specified. In contrast, selling a flock of sheep or a piece of cloth at a per-unit price without specifying the total is entirely invalid. This distinction is based on the nature of the items: grain is fungible (mithli) while sheep and cloth are non-fungible (qimi). Additionally, the passage covers the scenario when a heap is sold as a specific total and the actual quantity differs—the buyer has an option if short, and excess belongs to the seller.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three per-unit sale cases and their rulings
Case 1: Grain per qafiz → valid for one qafiz (Abu Hanifa). Case 2: Sheep per head → invalid entirely. Case 3: Cloth per cubit → invalid entirely. Note that for grain, the sale is partially valid; for others, it is completely fasid.
The ruling for grain is contingent on not specifying the total number of qafizes.
2
Recognize the illa: fungibility vs non-fungibility
Grain is fungible (mithli) — each qafiz is uniform and interchangeable. Sheep and cloth are non-fungible (qimi) — each animal or cubit may differ. The per-unit pricing without total creates ambiguity that is tolerable for fungibles but not for non-fungibles.
Abu Hanifa's reasoning: for grain, the price per unit is known, so at least one unit can be validly sold; the rest are unspecified.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the grain case
Abu Hanifa holds the sale valid for one qafiz. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) likely consider the entire sale fasid because the total is ambiguous. The matn gives prominence to Abu Hanifa's view.
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the buyer can take one qafiz and pay one dirham; under the Sahibayn, the entire transaction is void.
4
Apply the lump sum discrepancy rule
When a heap is sold as a specific total (e.g., 100 qafiz for 100 dirhams), if the actual quantity is less, the buyer has the option to take the existing amount at a proportionate price or cancel the sale. If it is more, the excess belongs to the seller without extra payment.
This rule is based on the principle that the seller implicitly guarantees the stated quantity; any shortage gives the buyer a choice, while any surplus is a gift to the seller.
5
Synthesize the underlying principle
In all these cases, the key is the specificity of the subject matter. When the total quantity is unknown, the sale is either partially valid (for fungibles) or wholly invalid (for non-fungibles). When the total is stated but differs, fairness is achieved through options or adjustment.
This demonstrates the Hanafi commitment to removing gharar while allowing practical flexibility.

Sales by Unit without Specifying Total Measure
Rulings on selling grain, sheep, and cloth per unit without naming total quantity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling a subrah per qafiz without naming total and distinguish the exception for one qafiz from the absolute invalidity for sheep and cloth, applying the underlying principle of gharar.

Core Idea
Selling by unit without total is invalid except for one qafiz of grain according to Abu Hanifa.

When a seller offers goods per unit – like grain per qafiz, sheep per head, or cloth per cubit – but does not state the total quantity, ambiguity arises. Hanafi law treats such sales with caution: a minimal exception exists for grain, but sheep and cloth are wholly invalid. This lesson examines the rulings and the reasons behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
Read each of the three cases: grain heap (subrah) sold per qafiz, flock of sheep sold per head, cloth sold per cubit. Note the ruling for each as stated in the text.
The grain sale is valid for one qafiz only (according to Abu Hanifa), while sheep and cloth sales are invalid (fasid) in their entirety.
2
Locate the Dalil
The primary proof is the hadith prohibiting the sale of a heap of dates without knowing its measure. Identify how this hadith is related to the rulings.
The hadith (Muslim, Bab Tahrim Bay' al-Subrah) is cited in the commentary.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
Explain how the prohibition against selling an unknown measure leads to the specific Hanafi position. Why does Abu Hanifa make an exception for one qafiz?
One qafiz is a known, minimal quantity, so the prohibition's cause (gharar) does not apply. For multiple qafiz, the total price is uncertain.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf
The phrase 'according to Abu Hanifa' indicates that the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) may hold a different view. What might their position be?
The text does not specify their view, but they likely consider the sale invalid even for one qafiz due to the total uncertainty.
5
Evaluate the Practical Consequence
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf: the difference in practice between relying on Abu Hanifa's exception versus the Sahibayn's stricter position.
The mufta bihi for general fatwa is to require full specification of total, making the exception rarely applied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ بَاعَ صُبْرَةَ طَعَامٍ كُلَّ قَفِيزِ بِدِرْهَم جَازَ الْبَيْعُ فِي قَفِيرِ وَاحِدٍ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ إِلَّا أَنْ يُسَمِّيَ جُمْلَةَ قُفْزَانِهَا وَمَنْ بَاعَ قَطِيعَ غَنَمٍ كُلَّ شَاةٍ بِدِرْهَم فَالْبَيْعُ فَاسِدٌ فِي جَمِيعِهَا وَكَذَلِكَ مَنْ بَاعَ ثَوْبًا مُذَارَعَةً كُلَّ ذِرَاعَ بِدِرْهَم وَلَمْ يُسَمَ جُمْلَةَ النُّرْعَانِ
Whoever sells a heap of food, each qafiz for a dirham, the sale is valid for one qafiz according to Abu Hanifa, unless he names the total number of its qafiz. Whoever sells a flock of sheep, each sheep for a dirham, the sale is invalid in all of them. Similarly, whoever sells cloth by the yard, each cubit for a dirham, and does not name the total number of cubits.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage presents three rulings on selling items by unit measure without specifying the total quantity. The first case involves a heap (subrah) of grain sold per qafiz (a volume measure). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the sale is valid only for a single qafiz, unless the seller explicitly mentions the total number of qafiz. This is because the transaction is considered permissible for the first unit as a minimal, known quantity, but ambiguity arises for multiple units since the total price is uncertain. For sheep sold per head and cloth sold per cubit, the sale is completely invalid (fasid) in all items, because each unit is discrete and the lack of a total creates gharar (uncertainty). The difference lies in the nature of the commodities: grain is homogeneous and typically sold by measure, so one unit is acceptable; sheep and cloth are heterogeneous in that each item is distinct, so uncertainty is more detrimental. The text implies that for a valid sale, either the total quantity must be specified or the transaction must be for a single unit only. The proof for the grain ruling is the hadith prohibiting the sale of a heap of dates without knowing its measure (Muslim), and for the sheep and cloth, the general prohibition of cheating (Abu Dawood).

Key Terms
صُبْرَة (subrah): A heap of grain or dates whose total measure is unknown.
قَفِيز (qafiz): A standard measure of volume for grain, varying by region.
مُذَارَعَة (mudhara'ah): Selling by the yard or cubit measure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling on selling a heap of grain (subrah) per qafiz without naming the total number of qafiz?
A) The sale is valid for any number of qafiz.
B) The sale is invalid for all qafiz.
C) The sale is valid only for one qafiz.
D) The sale is valid if the buyer agrees to take the entire heap.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, selling by unit without specifying total quantity is generally invalid due to gharar, but Imam Abu Hanifa makes an exception for one qafiz of grain.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Sale of Heap with Specified Measure: Shortfall and Excess
Ruling on buyer's option when a heap sold as a certain measure is found less or more.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a heap sold with a specified measure is found deficient or excessive, and apply the option (khiyar) accordingly.

Core Idea
A deficiency gives the buyer an option; an excess belongs to the seller without extra payment.

When a specific quantity is stipulated in a sale, both parties rely on that representation. If the actual amount differs, fairness requires adjustments. This lesson examines the Hanafi position when a heap (subrah) is sold as a particular measure but found to be either less or more. The rule balances protecting the buyer from loss and not unjustly enriching them at the seller's expense.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sale scenario
Understand that the sale is of a heap (subrah) with a stated measure (e.g., 100 qafiz) for a fixed price (100 dirhams). The measure is a condition of the sale.
The sale is valid initially (sahih).
2
Determine the actual quantity found
Two possibilities: the buyer finds the heap less than stated, or more than stated. Each triggers a different ruling.
3
Apply the ruling for deficiency
If less, the buyer has khiyar (option): either take the actual quantity at a proportionate price, or cancel the sale entirely.
The proportionate price is hissah – fraction of price corresponding to actual measure.
4
Apply the ruling for excess
If more, the excess belongs to the seller. The buyer pays only the agreed price and receives only the stated measure; the rest reverts to the seller.
The buyer does not get the excess for free, nor must they pay extra unless they agree.
5
Identify the underlying proof
The ruling is supported by the statement of 'Ikrimah (a tabi'i): 'If you buy food and find it in excess, the excess belongs to the seller of the food; the deficiency is against you.' This is considered a reliable transmitted proof (qawl sahabi).
The proof is zanni in transmission but provides clear guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ بَاعَ صُبْرَةَ طَعَامٍ عَلَى أَنَّهَا مِائَةُ قَفِيرٍ بِمِائَةِ دِرْهَم فَوَجَدَهَا أَقَلَّ فَالْمُشْتَرِي بِالْخِيَارِ إِنْ شَاءَ أَخَذَ الْمَوْجُودَ بِحِصَّتِهِ وَإِنْ شَاءَ فَسَخَ الْبَيْعَ وَإِنْ وَجَدَهَا أَكْثَرَ مِنْ ذَلِكَ فَالرِّيَادَةُ لِلْبَائِعِ
Whoever sells a heap of food on the condition that it is one hundred qafiz for one hundred dirhams, and finds it less, then the buyer has the option: if he wishes, he takes what is found at its proportionate price, and if he wishes, he cancels the sale. And if he finds it more than that, then the excess belongs to the seller.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling when a heap (subrah) of food is sold under the representation that it contains a specific measure (100 qafiz for 100 dirhams). If the buyer discovers the heap is actually less than stated, he has a choice (khiyar): he may either take the actual quantity at a price proportional to the agreed rate (i.e., pay for what he receives) or cancel the sale entirely due to deficiency. If the heap turns out to be more than stated, the excess quantity belongs to the seller without additional payment; the buyer gets exactly what he paid for. The rationale is that the sale was concluded on the basis of the specified total, so any excess is not part of the bargain. This rule protects both parties from gharar while ensuring fairness. The proof cited is the statement of 'Ikrimah: 'If you buy food and find it in excess, the excess belongs to the seller of the food; the deficiency is against you.'

Key Terms
خِيَار (khiyar): The option of the buyer in case of a deficiency in quantity; he may either take at proportionate price or cancel.
حِصَّة (hissah): Proportionate share; the price adjusted for the actual quantity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer purchases a heap of grain on the condition it is 100 qafiz for 100 dirhams, and finds it to be 110 qafiz, what happens?
A) The buyer takes all 110 qafiz for 100 dirhams.
B) The buyer must pay extra for the excess.
C) The excess (10 qafiz) belongs to the seller.
D) The sale is automatically canceled.

Key Takeaway
When a heap sold with a specified measure is deficient, the buyer has an option; when it is excessive, the surplus belongs to the seller.

intermediate
6 min
Sale of Cloth or Land with Specified Measure: Shortfall and Excess
Ruling on buyer's option when cloth or land sold as a specific measure is found less or more

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a sold cloth or land is found to be less or more than the specified measure, and apply the distinction between lump-sum and per-unit pricing.

Core Idea
If the measure is less, buyer has option; if more, buyer may get excess free or have option depending on pricing method.

In sales where a specific measure is guaranteed, a discrepancy between the stated and actual quantity can arise. The Hanafi school provides clear rules based on how the price was quoted: either as a lump sum for the total measure or as a per-unit rate. This lesson unpacks these rules, showing that the buyer's options differ depending on which pricing method was used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pricing method
Determine whether the seller quoted a total price for a total measure (lump sum) or a price per unit (e.g., per cubit).
The Arabic phrase 'بِعَشَرَةِ دَرَاهِمَ' indicates lump sum; 'كُلَّ ذِرَاعٍ بِدِرْهَم' indicates per-unit.
2
Check if actual measure is less or more
Compare the actual measure found with the measure specified in the contract.
3
Apply the lump-sum ruling
If lump sum and shortfall: buyer may take at full price or cancel. If excess: buyer gets excess free; seller has no option.
This is the first scenario in the text.
4
Apply the per-unit ruling
If per-unit and shortfall: buyer may take at proportionate price or cancel. If excess: buyer may take all at per-unit price or cancel.
This is the second scenario in the text.
5
Note the asymmetry
In both scenarios, the seller has no option to void the sale; only the buyer can exercise option (khiyar).
This protects the buyer from paying for un received goods and prevents the seller from profiting from uncertainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ اشْتَرَى ثَوْبًا عَلَى أَنَّهُ عَشَرَةُ أَذْرُعٍ بِعَشَرَةِ دَرَاهِمَ أَوْ أَرْضًا عَلَى أَنَّهَا مِائَةُ ذِرَاعٍ بِمِائَةِ دِرْهَم فَوَجَدَهُمَا أَقَلَّ مِنْ ذَلِكَ فَالْمُشْتَرِي بِالْخِيَارِ إِنْ شَاءَ أَخَذَهَا بِجُمْلَةِ الثَّمَنِ وَإِنْ شَاءَ تَرَكَ وَإِنْ وَجَدَهَا أَكْثَرَ مِنْ الذِرَاعِ الَّذِي سَمَّاهُ فَهُوَ لِلْمُشْتَرِي وَلَا خِيَارَ لِلْبَائِعِ وَإِنْ قَالَ بِعْتُكهَا عَلَى أَنَّهَا مِائَةُ ذِرَاعٍ بِمِائَةِ دِرْهَمٍ كُلَّ ذِرَاعٍ بِدِرْهَم فَوَجَدَهَا نَاقِصَةً فَهُوَ بالخيار إنْ شَاءَ أَخَذَهَا بِحِصَّتِهَا مِنْ الثَّمَن وَإِنْ شَاءَ تَرَكَهَا وَإِنْ وَجَدَهَا زَائِدَةً فَهُوَ بِالْخِيَارِ إِنْ شَاءَ أَخَذَ الْجَمِيعَ كُلَّ ذِرَاعٍ بِدِرْهَم وَإِنْ شَاءَ فَسَخَ الْبَيْعَ
Whoever buys cloth on the condition that it is ten cubits for ten dirhams, or land on the condition that it is one hundred cubits for one hundred dirhams, and finds them less than that, then the buyer has the option: if he wishes, he takes them with the entire price, and if he wishes, he leaves. And if he finds them more than the cubits he specified, then it belongs to the buyer, and the seller has no option. And if he says: 'I sold it to you on the condition that it is one hundred cubits for one hundred dirhams, each cubit for a dirham,' and he finds it deficient, then he has the option: if he wishes, he takes it at its proportionate share of the price, and if he wishes, he leaves it. And if he finds it in excess, then he has the option: if he wishes, he takes all, each cubit for a dirham, and if he wishes, he cancels the sale.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage addresses cases where cloth or land is sold with a specified total length/area and total price. The first scenario: the seller states the item is a specific total measure (e.g., 10 cubits of cloth for 10 dirhams) without per-unit pricing. If the actual measure is less, the buyer may either take it for the full price (i.e., accept the deficiency) or cancel. If it is more, the excess belongs to the buyer for free (no extra payment) and the seller cannot cancel. This is because the transaction was for a lump sum and the seller underestimated; the buyer gets the benefit. The second scenario: the seller explicitly states a per-unit price (e.g., each cubit for a dirham, with a total of 100 cubits for 100 dirhams). Here, if short, the buyer may take at proportional price (pay for what he gets) or cancel. If excess, the buyer has the option to take all at the per-unit price or cancel. This is because the per-unit price is fixed, so the total quantity is uncertain; giving both parties an option protects against imbalance. The proof for the second scenario is the hadith: 'There is no sale and loan combined, nor two conditions in one sale, nor profit of what is not guaranteed, nor sale of what you do not possess' (Tirmidhi), which underscores avoiding ambiguity.

Key Terms
ذِرَاع (dhira'): A cubit, a unit of length in pre-modern Islamic world.
جُمْلَةُ الثَّمَن (jumlat al-thaman): The total price, not per unit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a sale of cloth where the seller says 'I sold it to you as ten cubits for ten dirhams' (lump sum), and the buyer finds it to be eleven cubits, what is the ruling?
A) The buyer must pay extra for the extra cubit.
B) The buyer gets the extra cubit free and the seller has no option.
C) The buyer has the option to cancel or accept the extra at proportionate price.
D) The seller can void the sale.

Key Takeaway
In a lump-sum sale, the buyer gets any excess for free; in a per-unit sale, the buyer has an option on excess.

6 min
Inclusion of Buildings, Trees, Crops, and Fruit in Sale
Rulings on what is included automatically in sale of house, land, or trees

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi default rules for what is automatically included in a sale of a house, land, and trees, and apply the hadith to determine fruit ownership.

Core Idea
Buildings and trees follow the land; crops and fruit do not — unless stipulated.

When you buy a house, do you automatically get the building? When you buy land, do the trees come with it? The Hanafi school sets clear default rules based on custom and a Prophetic hadith. This lesson unpacks those defaults and their rationale from Al-Quduri's matn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Four Scenarios
The passage covers: sale of a house (building), sale of land (trees), sale of land (crops), and sale of trees (fruit).
2
Determine the Default for Each
Note which items are automatically included: building (included), trees (included), crops (excluded), fruit (excluded unless buyer stipulates).
3
Locate the Dalil
The hadith: 'Whoever buys palm trees after they have been pollinated, their fruit belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates.' (Muslim)
4
Apply the Hadith to the Fruit Case
The fruit default is seller's property; buyer can reverse it by explicit stipulation.
5
Note the Rationale
Buildings and trees are fixtures; crops are temporary and harvested; fruit is separate property until it becomes part of the tree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ بَاعَ دَارًا دَخَلَ بِنَاؤُهَا فِي الْبَيْعِ وَإِنْ لَمْ يُسَمِّهِ وَمَنْ بَاعَ أَرْضًا دَخَلَ مَا فِيهَا مِنْ النَّخْلِ وَالشَّجَرِ فِي الْبَيْعِ وَإِنْ لَمْ يُسَمِّهِ وَلَا يَدْخُلُ الزَّرْعُ فِي بَيْعِ الْأَرْضِ إِلَّا بِالتَّسْمِيَةِ وَمَنْ بَاعَ نَخَلًا أَوْ شَجَرًا فِيهِ ثَمَرَةٌ فَثَمَرَتُهُ لِلْبَائِعِ إِلَّا أَنْ يَشْتَرِطَهَا الْمُبْتَاعُ
Whoever sells a house, its building enters the sale even if he did not name it. Whoever sells land, the palm trees and other trees in it enter the sale even if he did not name them. And crops do not enter the sale of land except by specification. And whoever sells palm trees or trees which have fruit on them, the fruit belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates it.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage clarifies what is included automatically when selling immovable property. When selling a house, the building (the structure itself) is included by default; the buyer gets the house as is. When selling land, all trees growing on it (including palm trees) are included automatically, regardless of whether they are mentioned. This is because they are considered part of the land. However, standing crops (zara') are not included unless explicitly mentioned; this is because crops are typically harvested separately and are not considered a permanent fixture. When selling palm trees or other trees that have fruit on them, the fruit (thamar) belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates in the contract that he is buying the fruit as well. This ruling is based on the hadith of Abdullah ibn Umar: 'Whoever buys palm trees after they have been pollinated, their fruit belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates.' (Bukhari and Muslim). The rationale is that fruit is often considered separate property, and the tree can be sold without the fruit.

Key Terms
بِنَاء (bina'): The building or structure on a piece of land; considered part of the house sale automatically.
زَرْع (zar'): Crops growing on land; not automatically included in sale of land.
تَأْبِير (ta'bir): Pollination of palm trees; after this, fruit is considered separate property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone sells a piece of land with fruit-bearing trees, and no stipulation is made about the fruit, who owns the fruit?
A) The buyer owns the fruit.
B) The seller owns the fruit.
C) The fruit is divided between buyer and seller equally.
D) The fruit is not owned by anyone until harvested.

Key Takeaway
Buildings and trees are fixtures included in land sale; crops and fruit are separate and require explicit agreement.

Who Pays the Transaction Costs? The Hanafi Rule
States responsibility for transaction costs with two proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who bears the costs of measurement and price checking in a sale, and identify the evidentiary basis from the tabi'i precedent and the hadith on gharar.

Core Idea
The seller pays the measurer and the price checker; the buyer pays the weigher of the price.

This lesson from Al-Quduri's matn clarifies the default allocation of transaction costs in a sale. The Hanafi school assigns these fees to specific parties to prevent uncertainty (gharar) that could invalidate the contract. We examine the ruling and its two proofs: a direct precedent from the tabi'i al-Sha'bi and a broader hadith on gharar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The passage states a clear rule: the fee of the measurer (kayyāl) and the price checker (nāqid al-thaman) is upon the seller, and the fee of the weigher (wāzin al-thaman) is upon the buyer. This is a wajib (obligatory) allocation.
The word 'عَلَى' indicates obligation.
2
Examine the first proof: Tabi'i precedent
The narration from Wardan ibn Abi al-Nadr and al-Sha'bi establishes a direct precedent where al-Sha'bi ruled that the seller must pay the measurer.
Though not a hadith, this is considered a valid proof (qawl al-tabi'i) in Hanafi usul.
3
Examine the second proof: Hadith on gharar
The Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) prohibited selling gharar (uncertainty). This general principle implies that all terms of a sale must be clear, including who bears ancillary costs.
The connection is indirect: leaving fees unspecified could lead to dispute, which is a form of gharar.
4
Understand the legal link (wajh al-dalalah)
The hadith on gharar supports the rule by showing that uncertainty in financial obligations invalidates the sale. Assigning each fee to a specific party removes that uncertainty.
This is a form of istidlal: applying a general principle to a specific case.
5
Note cross-madhhab differences
Some other schools may assign all fees to the seller or buyer differently. The Hanafi ruling is relied upon (mufta bihi) and is part of zahir al-riwayah.
The text describes the ruling as mukhtalaf fih (disputed among schools).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأُجْرَةُ الْكَيَّالِ وَنَاقِدُ التَّمَنِ عَلَى الْبَائِعِ وَأَجْرَةُ وَازِنِ الثَّمَنِ عَلَى الْمُشْتَرِي وَجْهٌ : (۱) قَوْلُ التَّابِعِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ وَأُجْرَةُ الْكَيَّالِ وَنَاقِدُ الثَّمَنِ عَلَى الْبَائِعِ عَنْ وَرْدَانَ بْنِ أَبِي النَّضْرِ، قَالَ: كُنْتُ بِعْتُ مِن رَجُلٍ طَعَامًا، فَأَعْطَى الرَّجُلُ أَجْرَ الْكَيَّالِ فَسَأَلْتُ الشَّعْبِيَّ عَن ذَلِكَ، فَقَالَ: «أَعْطِهِ أَنْتَ فَإِنَّمَا هُوَ عَلَيْكَ» (مُصَنَّفُ ابْنِ أَبِي شَيْبَةَ، الرَّجُلُ يَبِيعُ الطَّعَامَ عَلَى مَنْ يَكُونُ أَجْرُ الْكَيَّالِ نُمْبِر ٢١٩٣٧) وَجْهٌ : (٢) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ وَأُجْرَةُ الْكَيَّالِ وَنَاقِدُ الثَّمَنِ عَلَى الْبَائِعِ عَنْ أَبِي هُرَيْرَةَ: «أَنَّ النَّبِيَّ نَهَى عَنْ بَيْعِ الْغَرَرِ زَادَ عُثْمَانُ وَالْحَصَاةِ» (سُنَنُ أَبِي دَاوُدَ، بَابٌ فِي بَيْعِ الْغَرَرِ، نُمْبِر ٣٣٧٦)
And the fee of the measurer and the examiner of the price is upon the seller, and the fee of the weigher of the price is upon the buyer. Proof (1): The saying of the follower (tabi'i) for establishing that the fee of the measurer and the examiner of the price is upon the seller: from Wardan ibn Abi al-Nadr, he said: I sold food to a man, and the man gave the measurer's fee, so I asked al-Sha'bi about that, and he said: 'Give it yourself, for it is upon you.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, no. 21937) Proof (2): The hadith for establishing that the fee of the measurer and the examiner of the price is upon the seller: from Abu Hurayra: the Prophet prohibited the sale of gharar; Uthman added: and the sale of pebbles. (Sunan Abi Dawud, no. 3376)
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This is a ruling from the matn of Al-Quduri specifying who bears transaction costs in a sale. The seller must pay the measurer (who measures the goods) and the person who checks the price (e.g., a tester of coins). The buyer must pay the one who weighs the price (e.g., the gold or silver). The first proof is a direct precedent from the tabi'i al-Sha'bi, who told the seller to pay the measurer. The second proof cites the general prohibition of gharar (uncertainty) to support that these costs should be clearly assigned to avoid dispute. The hadith text is clear, though its connection to the fee rule is indirect, indicating that leaving such costs undefined could lead to discord.

Key Terms
الكَيَّال (al-kayyāl): The person who measures the goods being sold (e.g., grain).
نَاقِدُ الثَّمَنِ (nāqid al-thaman): The person who examines the price (e.g., checks coins for purity or weight).
وَازِنُ الثَّمَنِ (wāzin al-thaman): The person who weighs the price (e.g., weighs silver or gold).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a sale, who is responsible for paying the measurer (al-kayyāl) according to the Hanafi school?
A) The seller
B) The buyer
C) Both equally
D) The person who hires the measurer

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, transaction costs are allocated to prevent gharar: the seller pays for measuring goods and checking price; the buyer pays for weighing the price.

Order of Delivery in a Cash Sale: Buyer Pays First
Specifies order: buyer pays first, then seller delivers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the sequence of delivery in a cash sale and identify the two hadith proofs that support it.

Core Idea
In a cash sale, the buyer must pay first; only then must the seller deliver.

This passage from Al-Quduri lays down a clear order in cash sales: the buyer must deliver the price first, and only after that must the seller deliver the goods. Two hadith proofs are cited: the Prophet's immediate payment to the cupper, and the riba hadith requiring hand-to-hand exchange. Understanding this sequence is essential for the validity of a cash sale contract and prevents disputes over non-performance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
The passage states that in a cash sale, the buyer must deliver the price first (wajib), then the seller must deliver the goods. This is based on the principle of immediate exchange.
The command 'qila' implies obligation, not recommendation.
2
Examine the first hadith proof
The hadith from Ibn Abbas: the Prophet cupped himself and gave the cupper his fee immediately. This shows that payment should not be delayed; in a sale, prompt payment is required from the buyer.
The hadith is from Sahih al-Bukhari, indicating strong authenticity.
3
Examine the second hadith proof
The riba hadith from Umar demands hand-to-hand exchange for gold, silver, and foodstuffs. By analogy (qiyas), this principle extends to all cash sales, reinforcing that the buyer's payment must be immediate.
Even though the hadith is about riba, it is used to establish immediate exchange in general.
4
Note the absence of dispute
This ruling is agreed upon among the schools (muttafaq alayh). There is no significant internal Hanafi disagreement.
The stated position is the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ بَاعَ سِلْعَةً بِثَمَنٍ قِيلَ لِلْمُشْتَرِي سَلِّمْ الثَّمَنَ أَوَّلًا فَإِذَا دَفَعَ الثَّمَنَ قِيلَ لِلْبَائِعِ سَلِّمْ الْمَبِيعَ وَجْهٌ : (۱) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ وَمَنْ بَاعَ سِلْعَةَ بِثَمَنٍ قِيلَ لِلْمُشْتَرِي عَنِ ابْنِ عَبَّاسٍ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُمَا قَالَ: «احْتَجَمَ النَّبِيُّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ وَأَعْطَى الْحَجَّامَ أَجْرَهُ» (صحيح البخاري، بَابُ خَرَاجِ الْحَجَّامِ، نُمْبِر ٢٢٧٨) وَجْهٌ (۲) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ وَمَنْ بَاعَ سِلْعَةً بِثَمَنٍ قِيلَ لِلْمُشْتَرِي عَنْ عُمَرَ، قَالَ: قَالَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ: «الذَّهَبُ بِالْوَرِقِ رِبًا إِلَّا هَاءَ وَهَاءَ، وَالْبُرُّ بِالْبُرِّ رِبًا إِلَّا هَاءَ وَهَاءَ، وَالتَّمْرُ بِالتَّمْرِ رِبًا إِلَّا هَاءَ وَهَاءَ، وَالشَّعِيرُ بِالشَّعِيرِ رِبًا إِلَّا هَاءَ وَهَاءَ» (سُنَنُ أَبِي دَاوُدَ، بَابٌ فِي الصَّرْفِ، نُمْبِر ٣٣٤٨)
And whoever sells goods for a price, it is said to the buyer: 'Deliver the price first.' Then when he has paid the price, it is said to the seller: 'Deliver the goods.' Proof (1): The hadith for establishing that whoever sells goods for a price, it is said to the buyer: from Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, who said: 'The Prophet cupped himself and gave the cupper his fee.' (Sahih al-Bukhari, no. 2278) Proof (2): The hadith for establishing that whoever sells goods for a price, it is said to the buyer: from Umar, he said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'Gold for silver is riba unless it is hand to hand; wheat for wheat is riba unless it is hand to hand; dates for dates is riba unless it is hand to hand; barley for barley is riba unless it is hand to hand.' (Sunan Abi Dawud, no. 3348)
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage regulates the order of performance in a cash sale. The buyer must first pay the price; only after payment does the seller deliver the goods. The first proof uses the hadith of the Prophet giving the cupper's fee immediately after the service, indicating that payment comes first. The second proof cites the riba hadith which demands hand-to-hand exchange; here, the buyer's payment is analogous to the initial delivery in a spot sale. Even though the second hadith is about riba, it underscores the necessity of immediate exchange without delay. This order ensures that the seller does not part with his goods without receiving payment.

Key Terms
سَلِّمْ (sallim): Command to hand over or deliver (the price or goods).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a cash sale, who must deliver first according to this passage?
A) The seller
B) The buyer
C) Both at once
D) Either party can choose

Key Takeaway
In a cash sale, the buyer must pay the price first before the seller delivers the goods.
