Sale of a Slave-Girl Excluding Her Pregnancy: Gharar and Fasid
Ruling on excluding the unborn child from a sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling a slave-girl except her pregnancy and explain why gharar renders the sale fasid.

Core Idea
Excluding an unknown pregnancy from a sale creates gharar, making the contract fasid in Hanafi fiqh.

This lesson tackles a classic sale involving gharar: a seller wishes to sell a pregnant slave-girl but retain the unborn child. The Hanafi school rules such a sale fasid because the pregnancy is an uncertain, unknown entity, and its exclusion introduces ambiguity into the contract. We will examine the ruling, its evidence from hadith, and the precise reason why this uncertainty invalidates the sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling a slave-girl while excluding her pregnancy. The ruling is that the sale is fasid (corrupt/voidable).
Fasid in Hanafi law means the contract is defective but can potentially be corrected.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a hadith from Jabir ibn Abdullah where the Prophet ﷺ prohibited muhaqalah, muzabanah, mukhabarah, and mu'awamah — all contracts involving gharar.
Mu'awamah is a multi-year sale of fruit, also called 'bay' al-sinin'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition of these sales indicates that any contract with significant uncertainty (gharar) in the subject matter or price is invalid. Analogically, excluding an unknown pregnancy creates similar uncertainty.
The pregnancy is uncertain in existence, viability, and timing, thus gharar.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school holds the sale batil (void ab initio), not fasid. Hanafi distinguishes between fasid (can be rectified) and batil (cannot).
The practical difference: in Hanafi, if the pregnancy is later separated, the sale of the mother may be salvaged.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is mufta bihi. The ruling is firmly established: selling with gharar renders the contract fasid, not automatically batil.
This is a case of gharar that can potentially be removed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ بَاعَ جَارِيَةً إِلَّا حَمْلَهَا فَالْبَيْعُ فَاسِدٌ

وَجْهٌ: (1) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ وَمَنْ بَاعَ جَارِيَةً إِلَّا حَمْلَهَا فَالْبَيْعُ فَاسِدٌ \ عَنْ جَابِرِ بْنِ عَبْدِ اللَّهِ، قَالَ: «نَهَى رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم عَنِ الْمُحَاقَلَةِ، وَالْمُزَابَنَةِ، وَالْمُخَابَرَةِ، وَالْمُعَاوَمَةِ»، قَالَ: عَنْ حَمَّادٍ، وَقَالَ: أَحَدُهُمَا وَالْمُعَاوَمَةِ وَقَالَ: الْآخَرُ بَيْعُ السِّنِينَ، ثُمَّ اتَّفَقُوا وَعَنِ الدُّنْيَا وَرَخَّصَ فِي الْعَرَايَا (سنن ابوداود شریف، باب فِي الْمُخَابَرَةِ، غمبر 3404)
And whoever sells a slave-girl except her pregnancy, the sale is fasid. Evidence: (1) The hadith for proof: From Jabir ibn Abdullah, he said: 'The Messenger of Allah ﷺ prohibited muhaqalah, muzabanah, mukhabarah, and mu'awamah.' ... (Sunan Abu Dawud, Kitab al-Mukhabarah, 3404)
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage addresses the sale of a slave-girl with a condition that the unborn child (pregnancy) is excluded from the sale. The author rules such a sale fasid. The proof is the general prohibition of certain sales (muhaqalah, muzabanah, etc.), which are contracts involving uncertainty (gharar). The pregnancy is unknown in its existence and development, making it gharar. In Hanafi fiqh, if the pregnancy is excluded, it becomes a separate future entity that cannot be sold independently due to gharar, and the main sale also becomes fasid because the contract includes an uncertain exception. The ruling categorically declares the sale fasid.

Key Terms
إلا حملها (illa hamlaha): Excluding the unborn child from the sale of the mother.
فاسد (fasid): A contract that is defective but can be corrected if the defect is removed.
غرر (gharar): Uncertainty or risk in a contract; prohibited when excessive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Selling a slave-girl while excluding her pregnancy results in a fasid sale due to the gharar inherent in the unknown pregnancy.

Sale with Tailoring Condition: A Fasid Contract
Ruling on selling cloth with tailoring condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a sale combined with a service condition and identify the hadith evidence that prohibits it.

Core Idea
A sale conditional on the seller's service is fasid because it combines two contracts in one, violating the hadith.

This passage from Al-Quduri deals with a common transaction: buying cloth on the condition that the seller also tailors it. While seemingly convenient, Hanafi fiqh considers this a defective sale (fasid) because it merges a sale with a hire (ijarah) into a single contract, leading to uncertainty and potential riba. The ruling is based on a prophetic hadith prohibiting 'two sales in one sale.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction and its ruling
The act is buying cloth (or leather) with a condition that the seller cuts, sews, or fashions it into a garment or shoes. The Hanafi ruling is that the sale is fasid (defective).
2
Locate the primary evidence (dalil)
The author cites a hadith from Abu Hurairah: 'Whoever conducts two sales in one sale, then he has the lesser of them or riba.' This is the proof for the prohibition.
Hadith from Sunan Abu Dawud and Tirmidhi.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof applies)
The phrase 'two sales in one sale' is interpreted broadly by Hanafis to include any combination of a sale with another contract, such as a hire (ijarah) for tailoring. Such combinations introduce gharar (uncertainty) regarding the price or compensation, potentially leading to riba.
Hanafi usul: general prohibition covers all forms of combining contracts.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (scholarly difference)
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may allow such a transaction if the service is clearly defined and the price is apportioned. However, Hanafis maintain it is fasid because the condition creates a second contract within the sale.
The practical difference: Hanafis require separate contracts.
5
Apply the ruling to similar scenarios
Any sale where the seller agrees to perform a separate service (e.g., tailoring, construction) as a condition of the sale is fasid. The buyer must either cancel the sale or renegotiate as two separate contracts.
If the sale has already been executed, it must be reversed if possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ اشْتَرَى ثَوْبًا عَلَى أَنْ يَقْطَعَهُ الْبَائِعُ وَيَخِيطَهُ قَمِيصًا، أَوْ قَبَاءً، أَوْ نَعْلًا عَلَى أَنْ يَحْذُوهَا، أَوْ يُشْرِكَهَا فَالْبَيْعُ فَاسِدٌ

وَجْهٌ: (1) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ وَمَنْ اشْتَرَى ثَوْبًا عَلَى أَنْ يَقْطَعَهُ الْبَائِعُ عَنْ أَبِي هُرَيْرَةَ، قَالَ: قَالَ النَّبِيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «مَنْ بَاعَ بَيْعَتَيْنِ فِي بَيْعَةٍ، فَلَهُ أَوْكَسُهُمَا أَوِ الرِّبَا» (سنن ابوداود شريف، بَابٌ فِيمَنْ بَاعَ بَيْعَتَيْنِ فِي بَيْعَةٍ، 3461/ سنن الترمذي، بَابُ مَا جَاءَ فِي النَّهْيِ عَنْ بَيْعَتَيْنِ فِي بَيْعَةٍ، 1231)
And whoever buys a cloth on condition that the seller cuts and sews it into a shirt, or a tunic, or sandals on condition that he fashions them, or shares with him, then the sale is fasid. Evidence: (1) The hadith for proof: From Abu Hurairah, he said: The Prophet ﷺ said: 'Whoever conducts two sales in one sale, then he has the lesser of them or riba.' (Sunan Abu Dawud, Kitab al-Buyu, 3461)
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage rules that if someone buys cloth with a condition that the seller will cut and sew it into a shirt or tunic, or buys leather with a condition that the seller will make it into shoes, then the sale is fasid. The author provides a hadith from Abu Hurairah that prohibits 'two sales in one sale' (بيعَتَين في بيعة). This refers to a contract that combines a sale with an additional service or condition that is separate from the sale. In Hanafi fiqh, the condition of tailoring is considered an additional contract of hire (ijarah) that should be separate. Combining them creates uncertainty (gharar) in the price or compensation. Hence, the sale is fasid. The ruling also applies if the seller says 'or shares' (أو يشركها), meaning a condition that the seller will have a share in the crafted item, which is also invalid.

Key Terms
بيعَتَين في بيعة (bay'atayn fi bay'ah): Combining two transactions into one contract, e.g., sale and hire.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It combines a sale with a service contract (hire) in one transaction.
C) The tailoring may not be done to satisfaction.
D) The price of the cloth and tailoring are unknown.

Key Takeaway
A sale conditional on the seller's service is fasid because it combines sale and hire, violating the prohibition of two sales in one.

Ruling on Sales Deferred to Unknown Festivals
Ruling on sales where the future due date is an unknown festival

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a sale deferred to an unknown festival and identify the reason (gharar/jahalah) that renders it fasid.

Core Idea
A sale deferred to an unknown festival is fasid due to uncertainty in the term.

In this lesson, we examine a specific type of deferred sale where the due date is tied to a festival whose exact date is unknown to the parties. The Hanafi school holds such sales invalid due to jahalah (ignorance of the term). We will explore the ruling, its underlying proof, and the principle that precise knowledge of the term is required for a valid deferred sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a sale where payment or delivery is deferred until a specific festival (Nawruz, Mihrajan, Christian fast, Jewish fast). The ruling is fasid (corrupt) if the parties do not know the exact date of that event.
The ruling applies only when both parties are ignorant of the precise date.
2
Locate the condition that triggers fasid
The passage explicitly states 'if the two contracting parties do not know that (the specific date)'. This jahalah in the term invalidates the contract.
Certainty of the due date is a condition for validity in deferred sales.
3
Identify the legal cause (illah)
The cause is gharar (uncertainty) arising from ignorance of the precise date. Gharar in the term (ajal) makes the sale fasid.
The Hanafi school is particularly strict about eliminating uncertainty in contracts.
4
Note the cited evidence
The author references a hadith from Ibn Abbas as proof, though the full text is not given. The principle is that fixing a date based on an unknown festival leads to prohibited gharar.
The hadith is ahad and considered zanni in transmission and meaning, but it supports the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْبَيْعُ إِلَى النَّيْرُوزِ، وَالْمِهْرَجَانِ وَصَوْمِ النَّصَارَى وَفِطْرِ الْيَهُودِ إِذَا لَمْ يَعْرِفْ الْمُتَبَايِعَانِ ذَلِكَ فَاسِدٌ

وَجْهٌ: (1) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ وَالْبَيْعُ إِلَى النَّيْرُوزِ، وَالْمِهْرَجَانِ \ عَنِ ابْنِ عَبَّاسٍ رَضِي الله عَنْهُمَا

أُصُولٌ: شَرِيعَتْ مِيْنَ مَقْصَدٌ مَيْ الگ الگ ہوں تو دو جنس ہے خواہ ایک ہی نسل کے ہوں، اور ایک نسل ہو اور مقصد بھی ایک ہو تو ایک جنس ہے۔
And the sale to Nawruz, Mihrajan, the fast of Christians, and the breaking of fast of Jews, if the two contracting parties do not know that (the specific date), is fasid. Evidence: (1) The hadith for proof: From Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them. Principle: In Shariah, if the purposes (maqasid) are different, they are two types even if from the same species, and if the species is one and the purpose is one, then it is one type.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage deals with sales where the payment or delivery is deferred to a date that is associated with non-Islamic festivals (Nawruz, Mihrajan, Christian fasts, Jewish fast-breaking). If the parties are ignorant of the actual calendar date of these events, the sale is fasid because the term is uncertain (jahalah). The author provides a brief hadith reference from Ibn Abbas. The accompanying usul (principle) note in Urdu explains that in Shariah, the categorization of goods (jins) depends on the maqasid (purposes/legal effects). If the purposes differ, even within the same species, they are considered separate types; if the purpose is identical, they are one type. This principle likely relates to rules of riba and exchange of different types.

Key Terms
النيروز (al-nayruz): Persian New Year festival, not an Islamic calendar date.
المهرجان (al-mihrajan): Another Persian festival.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a sale deferred to Nawruz or Mihrajan considered fasid?
A) Because these festivals are non-Islamic.
B) Because the exact day is unknown to both parties.
C) Because the buyer may forget the date.
D) Because the sale includes a condition.

Key Takeaway
A deferred sale is fasid if the due date is based on an uncertain festival date that the parties do not know.

Sale Deferred to Uncertain Harvest Seasons: Ruling and Proofs
Ruling on sale deferred to harvest or gathering time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a sale deferred to harvest seasons and explain the hadith proof that requires a known term.

Core Idea
A sale tied to an uncertain harvest season is fasid unless the parties cancel the term before the season begins.

In this passage, Al-Quduri rules on sales where payment is deferred to agricultural events like harvest or grape-picking. These events are considered uncertain because their exact timing varies. We'll examine the ruling, the exception, and the hadith evidence used to require a known fixed term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its general ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? A sale where payment is deferred to harvest, threshing, grape-picking, or arrival of Hajj. The ruling: it is not permissible (la yajuz).
The term 'not permissible' here indicates fasid (invalid due to uncertainty).
2
Note the exception
The text says: if both parties mutually agree to drop the deferment before the event begins, it becomes permissible. This shows the issue is the unknown term, not the contract itself.
The sale becomes a spot sale (cash and carry).
3
Analyze the hadith evidence
The Prophet ﷺ approved salam (advance payment) only with a known measure and known term. This implies that uncertain terms like harvest are not valid.
The hadith is from Ibn Abbas, narrated in Bukhari and Muslim.
4
Understand the companion statement
Ibn Abbas explicitly prohibits loans tied to harvest, threshing floor, pressing, etc., and instructs to set a clear term.
This reinforces the need for certainty in the term.
5
Recognize the cross-madhhab difference
Shafi'is also require a fixed term, while Malikis may allow if the season is generally known. The Hanafi position is strictest to avoid gharar.
This difference is a valid ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا يَجُوزُ الْبَيْعُ إِلَى الْحَصَادِ وَالدِّيَاسِ وَالْقِطَافِ وَقُدُومِ الْحَاجَ فَإِنْ تَرَاضَيَا بِإِسْقَاطِ الْأَجَلِ قَبْلَ أَنْ يَأْخُذَ النَّاسُ فِي الْحَصَادِ وَالدِّيَاسِ، وَالْقِطَافِ وَقُدُومِ الْحَاجَ جَازَ

وَجْهٌ: (1) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ وَلَا يَجُوزُ الْبَيْعُ إِلَى الْحَصَادِ وَالدِّيَاسِ وَالْقِطَافِ \ عَنِ ابْنِ عَبَّاسِ الله عنهما قَالَ: «قَدِمَ النَّبِيُّ الْهَلَال الْمَدِينَةَ وَهُمْ يُسْلِفُونَ فِي الثَّمَارِ السَّنَتَيْنِ وَالثَّلَاثَ، فَقَالَ: أَسْلِفُوا فِي القِمَارِ فِي كَيْلِ مَعْلُومٍ، إِلَى أَجَلٍ مَعْلُومٍ» (بخاري شريف بَابُ السَّلَمِ إِلَى أَجَلٍ مَعْلُومٍ، نمبر 2253/ مسلم شریف: باب السلم) ،نمبر 1604)

وَجْهٌ: (2) قَوْلُ الصَّحَابِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ وَلَا يَجُوزُ الْبَيْعُ إِلَى الْحَصَادِ وَالدِّيَاسِ وَالْقِطَافِ \ عَنِ ابْنِ عَبَّاسٍ قَالَ: " لَا سَلَفَ إِلَى الْعَطَاءِ، وَلَا إِلَى الْحَصَادِ، وَلَا إِلَى الْأَنْدَرِ، وَلَا إِلَى الْعَصِيرِ، وَاضْرِبْ لَهُ أَجَلًا ( السنن الكبري لليبيهقي، بَابُ لَا يَجُوزُ السَّلَفُ حَتَّى يَكُونَ بِثَمَنٍ مَعْلُومٍ فِي كَيْلٍ مَعْلُومٍ أَوْ وَزْنِ مَعْلُومٍ إِلَى أَجَلٍ وَلا يَخْتَلِفُ إِنْ كَانَ إِلَى أَجَلٍ مَعْلُومٍ غمبر (11115)
And the sale to harvest, threshing, grape-picking, and the arrival of the Hajj is not permissible. But if they mutually agree to drop the term before people start harvesting, threshing, grape-picking, and the arrival of the Hajj, it becomes permissible. Evidence: (1) The hadith for proof: From Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, he said: 'The Prophet ﷺ came to Medina and they were advancing payment for fruits for two or three years, so he said: Advance payment for fruit in a known measure and a known term.' (Bukhari, Kitab al-Salam, 2253; Muslim) Evidence: (2) The statement of the Companion: From Ibn Abbas, he said: 'No loan to 'ata', nor to harvest, nor to threshing floor, nor to pressing, and set a term for him.' (Baihaqi, al-Sunan al-Kubra, 11115)
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage continues the theme of sales with uncertain future terms. It rules that a sale (especially salam or deferred payment) is not valid if the due date is tied to agricultural events like harvest (hasad), threshing (diyas), grape-picking (qitaf), or the arrival of the Hajj caravans. These are considered uncertain because the exact time is not fixed. However, the ruling includes an exception: if the parties mutually agree to drop the deferred term before the event occurs and the sale becomes immediate, it becomes permissible. The author provides two pieces of evidence. First, a hadith from Ibn Abbas where the Prophet approved of salam (advance payment) only with a known measure and a known term, implying that uncertain terms like seasons are not allowed. Second, a statement of Ibn Abbas himself prohibiting loans (salf) tied to harvest, threshing floor, pressing, etc., and instructing to set a clear term. In Hanafi fiqh, this ruling ensures that the term is fixed and known to avoid gharar.

Key Terms
الحصاد (al-hasad): Harvest time, not a fixed date.
السلم (al-salam): Advance payment sale where price is paid now, delivery later.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition does a sale deferred to harvest become permissible?
A) If the buyer pays a higher price.
B) If both parties agree to cancel the deferment before harvest begins.
C) If the seller guarantees delivery.
D) If the harvest is expected within a month.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school invalidates sales with uncertain terms like harvest seasons, but permits them if the parties cancel the deferment before the event.

Possession in a Fasid Sale: Ownership and Liability
Ruling on possession in a fasid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on possession in a fasid sale and explain its consequences including ownership transfer and obligation of market value.

Core Idea
Possession with consent in a fasid sale transfers ownership but obligates market value.

This passage from Al-Quduri addresses a key Hanafi principle: that a fasid (voidable) sale, though defective, can still transfer ownership upon possession with the seller's permission. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its conditions, and the proof from the hadith of the woman who fed the sheep to prisoners.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (possession in a fasid sale) and what the ruling is (ownership transfers, market value owed, each party may rescind, subsequent sale is valid).
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited: the hadith from Sunan Abu Dawud 3332 where the Prophet instructed the woman to feed the sheep to prisoners, implying ownership despite the defective transaction.
Hadith is ahad, zanni in transmission and meaning.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the hadith proves the ruling: the Prophet's order to feed the sheep indicates the woman owned it, so the defective sale transferred ownership upon possession.
The word 'aṭʿimīhi' (feed it) is a command that implies ownership.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (cross-madhhab)
Contrast with Shafi'i view: in a batil sale, possession does not transfer ownership. Hanafi distinguishes fasid from batil; fasid transfers ownership but obligates value.
Also intra-Hanafi: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad agree on ownership transfer but differ on details of value calculation.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is from Zahir al-Riwayah: ownership transfers and market value is due. Both parties maintain rescission rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا قَبَضَ الْمُشْتَرِي الْمَبِيعَ فِي الْبَيْعِ الْفَاسِدِ بِإِذْنِ الْبَائِعِ – وَفِي الْعَقْدِ عِوَضَانِ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا هُوَ مَالٌ - مَلَكَ الْمَبِيعَ وَلَزِمَتْهُ قِيمَتُهُ وَلِكُلّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْ الْمُتَعَاقِدَيْنِ فَسْخُهُ وَإِنْ بَاعَهُ الْمُشْتَرِي نَفَذَ بَيْعُهُ

وَجْهٌ: (1) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ وَإِذَا قَبَضَ الْمُشْتَرِي الْمَبِيعَ فِي الْبَيْعِ الْفَاسِدِ \ عَنْ رَجُلٍ، مِنَ الْأَنْصَارِ، قَالَ: خَرَجْنَا مَعَ رَسُولِ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم فِي جَنَازَةٍ، .... فَأَرْسَلَتِ الْمَرْأَةُ، قَالَتْ: يَا رَسُولَ اللَّهِ، صلى الله عليه وسلم إِنِّي أَرْسَلْتُ إِلَى الْبَقِيعِ يَشْتَرِي لِي شَاةً فَلَمْ أَجِدْ فَأَرْسَلْتُ إِلَى جَارٍ لِي قَدِ اشْتَرَى شَاةً، أَنْ أَرْسِلْ إِلَيَّ بِمَا بِثَمَنِهَا، فَلَمْ يُوجَدْ، فَأَرْسَلْتُ إِلَى امْرَأَتِهِ فَأَرْسَلَتْ إِلَى بِمَا، فَقَالَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «أَطْعِمِيهِ الْأُسَارَى»، سنن ابوداود شريف بَابٌ في اجْتِنَابِ الشُّبُهَاتِ، نمبر 3332
And when the buyer takes possession of the sold item in a fasid sale with the seller's permission – and in the contract there are two compensations, each of which is property – he owns the sold item and its value becomes obligatory upon him, and each of the two contracting parties has the right to rescind it, and if the buyer resells it, his sale is effective. Evidence: (1) The hadith for proof: From a man of the Ansar, he said: We went out with the Messenger of Allah ﷺ for a funeral... then the woman sent a message: O Messenger of Allah, I sent to al-Baqi' to buy a sheep for me but I did not find one, so I sent to a neighbor who had bought a sheep that he send me what he bought with its price, but he was not found, so I sent to his wife and she sent something. The Messenger of Allah ﷺ said: 'Feed it to the prisoners.' (Sunan Abu Dawud, Kitab Ijtinab al-Shubuhat, 3332)
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage explains the consequences of a fasid (voidable) sale once the buyer has taken possession of the item with the seller's consent. It states that if the contract involves two compensations (i.e., price and commodity) both of which are property, then upon possession, the buyer becomes the owner of the item, but he must pay its market value (qimat) at the time of possession. Each party has the right to rescind the contract (fasakh), meaning they can cancel it. If the buyer resells the item to a third party, that subsequent sale is valid (nafidh). The hadith cited involves a woman who essentially took possession of a sheep through a dubious transaction, and the Prophet ordered her to feed it to prisoners, implying ownership was transferred despite the defect. In Hanafi fiqh, this ruling is based on the principle that possession in a fasid sale with mutual consent transfers ownership, but the price is determined by value, not the agreed price. The right of rescission remains with both parties.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Taking physical possession of the sold item.
قيمة (qimah): Market value at the time of possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fasid sale, after the buyer takes possession with the seller's permission, what is the buyer obligated to pay?
A) The price agreed in the contract
B) The market value at the time of possession
C) Nothing, the sale is void
D) A fine to the authorities

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, possession with permission in a fasid sale transfers ownership but obligates the market value, not the agreed price, while both parties may rescind.

Combining Permissible and Impermissible Items in One Sale
Ruling on combining two different items in one sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling (batil) when a lawful and an unlawful item are combined in one sale, and explain why the entire contract is void.

Core Idea
Combining a valid item with an invalid item in one sale voids the entire contract.

In Islamic commercial law, the subject matter of a sale must be a valid commodity. What happens when a seller bundles a permissible item with an impermissible one? The Hanafi school rules that the entire contract becomes void (batil), not merely the invalid portion. This lesson examines the classic example from Al-Quduri's Matn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act
The act is selling two items together in a single contract: one valid and one invalid.
2
Recognize the Invalid Items
A free person (hurr) cannot be sold; a dead animal (maytah) is impure and not a commodity.
Slaves and slaughtered sheep are valid, but the free person and dead animal are not.
3
State the Ruling
The entire sale is batil (void) – not just the invalid portion.
Hanafi law does not allow partial validity when the price is not apportioned.
4
Understand the Rationale
Since the contract is a single exchange, mixing valid and invalid items creates uncertainty (gharar) and includes an unlawful subject matter.
The price cannot be allocated to each item separately.
5
Note the Authority
This is the ruling from Zahir al-Riwayah, the authoritative body of Hanafi law.
Some other schools may differ, but the mufta bihi is batil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ جَمَعَ بَيْنَ حُرٍ وَعَبْدِ، أَوْ بَيْنَ شَاةٍ ذَكِيَّةٍ وَمَيِّتَةٍ بَطَلَ الْبَيْعُ فِيهِمَا جَمِيعًا
And whoever combines between a free person and a slave, or between a slaughtered sheep and a dead one, the sale is void (batil) in both of them together.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: if someone sells in one contract a free person (who cannot be sold) together with a slave, or sells a ritually slaughtered (halal) sheep together with a dead sheep (which is impure and not saleable), the entire sale is batil (void). The reason is that the contract combines a lawful item with an unlawful item in a single transaction, and since the price is not apportioned to each, the entire sale becomes void. In Hanafi fiqh, a free person is not a commodity; selling him is invalid. A dead animal is also not a valid commodity. Mixing them with valid items in one price without allocation invalidates the whole sale. This ruling differs from fasid (voidable) sales; here it is batil (void ab initio) because the subject matter includes something legally non-existent as property.

Key Terms
حر (hurr): A free person, not eligible for sale.
ميتة (maytah): A dead animal not slaughtered properly, impure and not saleable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a sale that combines a free person with a slave in one contract?
A) The sale is fasid for the free person but valid for the slave.
B) The entire sale is batil (void).
C) The sale is valid if the free person is not knowledgeable.
D) The sale is valid if the price is apportioned.

Key Takeaway
Combining a valid commodity with an invalid non-commodity in a single sale voids the entire contract in Hanafi fiqh.

Sale with Indefinite Term: Invalid Due to Gharar
Ruling on sales with ambiguous future terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sales with indefinite terms and apply the condition that the term must be known (ma'lum) for validity.

Core Idea
A deferred sale is invalid if the term is uncertain, even if both parties are satisfied.

This lesson examines a clear ruling from Al-Quduri's matn on sales with ambiguous future terms like 'until harvest'. You will learn why such sales are considered invalid (fasid) in the Hanafi school due to gharar (uncertainty), and what remedy exists if the parties wish to proceed. The proof from a hadith of Ibn Abbas reinforces the requirement that the term must be precisely known.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is a sale (bai') with a deferred payment tied to an indefinite future event like harvest (hasad), threshing (diyas), grape-picking (qitaf), or arrival of pilgrims (qudum al-hajj). The ruling is that such a sale is not permissible (la yajuz) – it is fasid (invalid) due to gharar.
Focus on the principle: uncertainty in the term invalidates the contract.
2
Understand the exception
If both parties mutually agree (taradaya) to drop the term (isqat al-ajal) before the specified event occurs, the sale becomes ja'iz (valid). This shows that the invalidity is not absolute but correctable by removing the uncertainty.
The exception highlights that the defect is the term itself, not the underlying contract.
3
Examine the proof (hadith)
Ibn Abbas narrated that when the Prophet (ﷺ) arrived in Medina, people were paying in advance for fruits for two or three years. He commanded: 'Pay in advance for fruits in a known measure and a known term (ila ajal ma'lum).' The command implies that an unknown term is not permissible.
The hadith is agreed upon (muttafaq alayh) – Bukhari and Muslim.
4
Apply the dalil to the ruling
The Prophet's explicit condition 'known term' (ajal ma'lum) establishes that any term not precisely defined (like harvest) is excluded. Therefore, the prohibition is derived from this demand for certainty.
The Hanafi school treats this as a strict textual requirement; urf (custom) does not override it.
5
Identify the legal basis (gharar)
The underlying cause (illa) for invalidity is gharar – uncertainty regarding the time of payment. This uncertainty can lead to disputes and is prohibited in sales. The remedy is to make the term definite or eliminate the deferment entirely.
This principle applies broadly: any uncertain condition in a contract may render it fasid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا يَجُوزُ الْبَيْعُ إِلَى الْحَصَادِ وَالدِّيَاسِ وَالْقِطَافِ وَقُدُومِ الْحَاجَ فَإِنْ تَرَاضَيَا بِإِسْقَاطِ الْأَجَلِ قَبْلَ أَنْ يَأْخُذَ النَّاسُ فِي الْحَصَادِ وَالدِّيَاسِ وَالْقِطَافِ وَقُدُومِ الْحَاجَ جَازَ. عَنِ ابْنِ عَبَّاسٍ قَالَ: «قَدِمَ النَّبِيُّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ الْمَدِينَةَ وَهُمْ يُسْلِفُونَ فِي الثَّمَارِ السَّنَتَيْنِ وَالثَّلَاثَ، فَقَالَ: أَسْلِفُوا فِي الثَّمَرِ فِي كَيْلٍ مَعْلُومٍ، إِلَى أَجَلٍ مَعْلُومٍ» (متفق عليه)
And a sale is not valid until harvest, threshing, grape-picking, or the arrival of the pilgrims. Then if they mutually agree to drop the term before the people begin harvest, threshing, grape-picking, or the arrival of the pilgrims, it is permissible. From Ibn Abbas: 'The Prophet, peace be upon him, came to Medina and they were paying in advance for fruits for two or three years. He said: Pay in advance for fruits in a known measure and a known term.' (Agreed upon)
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage from Al-Quduri's matn states a ruling on sales with an indefinite future term. The Hanafi position is that a sale with a term like 'until harvest' is invalid because the exact time is unknown, leading to uncertainty (gharar). However, if both parties agree to remove the time condition before the event occurs, the sale becomes valid. The proof is a hadith from Ibn Abbas where the Prophet allowed salaf (advance payment) only when the term and measure are clearly defined, implying that indefinite terms are prohibited. The key principle is that the term (ajal) must be ma'lum (known) for validity.

Key Terms
الْأَجَل (al-ajal): The fixed term or due date in a sale contract.
الْحَصَاد (al-hasad): Harvest time – an indefinite future event.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man sells his harvest as soon as the wheat is cut (ilā al-ḥaṣād). Is this sale valid according to the Hanafi school?
A) Valid because the buyer knows the crop is ready.
B) Invalid because the term is uncertain (gharar).
C) Valid if the price is paid in advance.
D) Invalid only if delivery is delayed.

Key Takeaway
A deferred sale with an indefinite term is invalid (fasid) due to gharar; the term must be known (ma'lum) and if the indefinite condition is removed before the event, the sale becomes valid.

Selling a Slave with a Mudabbir
Ruling on selling a mudabbir together with another slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling a mudabbir together with a regular slave and identify the dalil from the Prophet's practice.

Core Idea
A mudabbir is still property; combining him with another slave in a sale is valid for the non-mudabbir slave.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario in sales law: what happens when a seller combines a mudabbir (a slave promised freedom upon the master's death) with a regular slave in one contract. Unlike combining a free person or dead animal—which voids the entire sale—the mudabbir is treated as property, so the sale remains valid for the regular slave with an apportioned price. The ruling is grounded in a sound hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text covers two cases: (1) combining a regular slave with a mudabbir, and (2) combining one's own slave with another person's slave (with permission). Focus on the first.
2
State the ruling
For both cases, the sale is valid only for the slave that is fully owned (the regular slave or one's own slave), and the price is divided proportionally.
The mudabbir remains unsold because he is still property but the sale is not voided.
3
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith of Jabir in Sahih al-Bukhari: 'The Prophet, peace be upon him, sold the mudabbir.'
This shows that a mudabbir is saleable, so combining does not invalidate the other item.
4
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's action (fi'l) indicates permissibility. Since he sold a mudabbir alone, selling it with another item is also valid for the lawful part.
Hanafi usul: fi'l al-nabi is a proof (dalil) for permissibility unless shown otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ جَمَعَ بَيْنَ عَبْدٍ وَمُدَبَّرٍ، أَوْ بَيْنَ عَبْدِهِ وَعَبْدِ غَيْرِهِ صَحَّ فِي الْعَبْدِ بِحِصَّتِهِ مِنَ الثَّمَنِ. عَنْ جَابِرٍ قَالَ: «بَاعَ النَّبِيُّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ الْمُدَبَّرَ» (صحيح البخاري)
And if he combines between a slave and a mudabbar (a slave promised freedom upon owner's death), or between his own slave and another's slave, the sale is valid for the slave with his portion of the price. From Jabir: 'The Prophet, peace be upon him, sold the mudabbar.' (Sahih al-Bukhari)
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This ruling contrasts with the previous one: a mudabbir (slave whose freedom is contingent on the master's death) is still property, so combining him with a regular slave in one sale is valid for the regular slave, who is sold for his portion of the total price. Similarly, selling one's own slave along with another's slave (with permission) is valid for one's own slave. The proof is the hadith that the Prophet himself sold a mudabbir, indicating that such a slave is saleable. The key difference: a mudabbir is property (mal) like a regular slave, whereas a free person or dead animal is not. So the sale is not void but valid for the lawful items, allocating the price proportionally.

Key Terms
الْمُدَبَّرُ (al-mudabbir): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death (tadbir).
بِحِصَّتِهِ مِنَ الثَّمَنِ (bi-hissatihi min al-thaman): For his proportionate share of the total price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary dalil for the Hanafi ruling that a mudabbir can be sold?
A) A Quranic verse on contracts
B) The hadith of Jabir that the Prophet sold a mudabbir
C) Consensus (ijma) of the companions
D) Analogy (qiyas) to selling a regular slave

Key Takeaway
A mudabbir is still property; combining him with a slave in a sale does not invalidate the sale of the slave, as proven by the Prophet's own practice.

Prohibited Sales: Najsh, Saum, Talaqqi, and Hadir
Rulings on four prohibited sales practices

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the four prohibited sales practices and state the Hanafi ruling on each, citing the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
The Prophet forbade deceitful market practices to protect fair trade.

This passage from al-Quduri lists four sales practices explicitly prohibited by the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam). While these may seem like minor infractions, they are considered major prohibitions (makruh tahrimi or haram) in the Hanafi school. Understanding the precise definitions and the underlying rationale helps you avoid transactions that harm market integrity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited act
Name each term and its English translation. For example, najsh = bidding up with no intent to buy; sawm 'ala sawm akhih = outbidding a brother's negotiation; talaqqi al-jalb = intercepting traders before market; bay' al-hadir li-l-badi = city-dweller brokering for a villager.
Memorize the Arabic terms and their concise meanings.
2
Locate the hadith proof
For each practice, identify which hadith is cited. The passage mentions Ibn Umar for najsh, Abu Huraira for multiple, Anas for a bidding example, and Abu Huraira again for talaqqi.
All hadith are from the agreed upon collections (Bukhari and Muslim) except Anas from Tirmidhi.
3
Determine the ruling level
The explicit prohibition (nahy) indicates haram in Hanafi usul. However, the school often grades these as makruh tahrimi (prohibited, close to haram). The act itself is sinful.
Makruh tahrimi is the operative level for fatwa.
4
Understand the legal consequence
Despite the prohibition, the resulting sale is valid (sahih) but the perpetrator is sinful. The victim may have the right to rescind (khiyar al-ghabn) due to deception.
This nuance distinguishes between moral prohibition and contract invalidity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَنَهَى رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ عَنِ النَّجْشِ وَعَنِ السَّوْمِ عَلَى سَوْمِ أَخِيهِ وَعَنْ تَلَقِي الْجَلَبِ وَبَيْعِ الْحَاضِرِ لِلْبَادِي. عَنِ ابْنِ عُمَرَ قَالَ: «نَهَى النَّبِيُّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ عَنِ النَّجْشِ» (متفق عليه). عَنْ أَبِي هُرَيْرَةَ قَالَ: «نَهَى رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ أَنْ يَبِيعَ حَاضِرٌ لِبَادٍ، وَلَا تَنَاجَشُوا، وَلَا يَبِيعُ الرَّجُلُ عَلَى بَيْعِ أَخِيهِ...» (متفق عليه). عَنْ أَنَسٍ: «...فَقَالَ النَّبِيُّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ: مَنْ يَزِيدُ عَلَى دِرْهَمٍ؟» (سنن الترمذي). عَنْ أَبِي هُرَيْرَةَ: «نَهَى النَّبِيُّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ عَنِ التَّلَقِّي» (متفق عليه).
And the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, forbade najsh (bidding up without intent to buy), and forbade outbidding (bidding over a brother's bid) and forbade meeting caravans (to intercept traders before market), and forbade an urban dweller selling for a desert dweller (as a broker). From Ibn Umar: 'The Prophet forbade najsh.' (Agreed upon). From Abu Huraira: 'The Messenger of Allah forbade an urban dweller selling for a desert dweller, and do not practice najsh, and do not sell over a brother's sale...' (Agreed upon). From Anas: '...the Prophet said: Who will increase over a dirham?' (Tirmidhi). From Abu Huraira: 'The Prophet forbade meeting caravans.' (Agreed upon).
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage lists four prohibited sales practices. (1) Najsh: bidding up the price with no intention to buy, to deceive others. (2) Saum 'ala saum akhih: offering to buy a product after another person has already negotiated a price, undercutting his deal. (3) Talaqqi al-jalb: going out to meet incoming traders before they reach the market to buy cheap and resell at market price. (4) Bay' al-hadir li-l-badi: an urban resident acting as a broker for a rural visitor without payment, often exploiting the visitor's ignorance. All these are prohibited based on multiple hadith. The Hanafi school considers them makruh tahrimi (closer to haram) due to the strong prohibition. The usul principle attached: causing harm or defrauding others is disapproved (makruh). The key rationale is protecting market integrity and preventing deception.

Key Terms
النَّجْش (al-najsh): Bidding up the price of an item with no intention to buy, to trick others into paying more.
السَّوْم عَلَى سَوْم أَخِيهِ (al-sawm 'ala sawm akhih): Interfering in a pending sale by offering a better price after a buyer has already negotiated.
تَلَقِّي الْجَلَب (talaqqi al-jalb): Going out to meet incoming traders before they reach the market to buy goods directly, depriving the market.
بَيْع الْحَاضِر لِلْبَادِي (bay' al-hadir li-l-badi): A city resident acting as a broker for a villager without payment, often resulting in exploitation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is 'najsh' in sales?
A) Selling an item before receiving it
B) Bidding up the price without intending to buy
C) Selling by auction
D) Buying on credit

Key Takeaway
Four market practices are prohibited due to deception; the contracts remain valid but the perpetrators sin.

Prohibited but Valid: External Prohibitions in Sale Contracts
Lists prohibited sale practices and rules they do not invalidate the contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify prohibited practices in sales that do not invalidate the contract and explain the Hanafi principle distinguishing external prohibitions from internal defects.

Core Idea
Not every prohibited act in a sale invalidates the contract — only defects in essential elements do.

This passage from al-Quduri's matn presents several practices that are clearly prohibited by the Prophet (saws) and the Quran, yet the Hanafi school holds that the sale itself remains valid. Understanding why these prohibitions do not affect validity is key to mastering the Hanafi theory of contracts. The author also includes a ruling on combining different items in one sale, supported by a hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited acts listed
List each practice: najsh, bidding over brother's bid, talaqqi al-jalab, town dweller selling for Bedouin, sale during Friday adhan. Note also the ruling on combining a slave and mudabbar or two slaves.
2
Examine the evidence for each prohibition
The author cites ahadith for najsh, bidding over brother's bid, talaqqi, and town dweller/Bedouin, and a Quranic verse for sale at Friday adhan. Note that each prohibition is firmly established.
3
Recognize the key phrase: 'la yafsudu bihi al-bay'
Despite the strong prohibitions, the author concludes 'all that is disliked and the sale is not invalidated by it.' This is the central ruling.
4
Understand the Hanafi principle
The prohibition is external (haraji) — it does not affect the contract's essential elements (offer, acceptance, subject matter, price). Only internal defects like riba or gharar invalidate.
5
Apply the principle to the combination ruling
Selling one's own slave together with another's slave is valid for the owner's slave for his portion of the price, because the subject matter is partially owned. The contract is not wholly invalid; it operates proportionally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَابُ الْبَيْعِ الْفَاسِدِ وَإِنْ جَمَعَ بَيْنَ عَبْدٍ وَمُدَبَّرٍ، أَوْ بَيْنَ عَبْدِهِ وَعَبْدِ غَيْرِهِ صَحَ فِي الْعَبْدِ بِحِصَّتِهِ مِنْ الثَّمَنِ. عَنْ جَابِرٍ قَالَ: «بَاعَ النَّبِيُّ الْمُدَبَّرَ» (بخاري شريف، بَابُ بَيْعِ الْمُدَبَّرِ، نمبر 2230). وَنَهَى رَسُولُ اللَّهِ - صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ - عَنْ النَّجْشِ وَعَنْ السَّوْمِ عَلَى سَوْمِ أَخِيهِ. عَنِ ابْنِ عُمَرَ قَالَ: «نَهَى النَّبِيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم عَنِ النَّجْشِ» (بخاري شريف بَابُ النَّجْشِ، 2142/مسلم شریف1516). عَنْ أَبِي هُرَيْرَةَ قَالَ: «نَهَى رَسُولُ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أَنْ يَبِيعَ حَاضِرٌ لِبَادٍ، وَلَا تَنَاجَشُوا وَلَا يَبيعُ الرَّجُلُ عَلَى بَيْعِ أَخِيهِ، وَلَا يَخْطُبُ عَلَى خِطْبَةِ أَخِيهِ، وَلَا تَسْأَلُ الْمَرْأَةُ طَلَاقَ أُخْتِهَا لِتَكْفَأَ مَا فِي إِنَائِهَا.» (بخاري شريف بَابٌ: لَا يَبِيعُ عَلَى بَيْعِ أَخِيهِ، 2140/ مسلم شريف 1515). وَعَنْ تَلَقِي الْجَلَبِ وَبَيْعِ الْحَاضِرِ لِلْبَادِي. عَنْ أَبِي هُرَيْرَةَ قَالَ: «نَهَى النَّبِيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم عَنِ التَّلَقَّي، وَأَنْ يَبِيعَ حَاضِرٌ لِبَادٍ.» (بخاري شريف، بَابُ النَّهْي عَنْ تَلَقِي الرَّكْبَانِ، 2162/ مسلم شریف 1519). وَعَنْ الْبَيْع عِنْدَ أَذَانِ الْجُمُعَةِ. قَالَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى: ﴿يَتَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِذَا نُودِيَ لِلصَّلَاةِ مِن يَوْمِ الْجُمُعَةِ فَاسْعَوْا إِلَى ذِكْرِ اللَّهِ وَذَرُوا الْبَيْعَ﴾ (سورة الجمعة، 62:9). وَكُلُّ ذَلِكَ يُكْرَهُ وَلَا يَفْسُدُ بِهِ الْبَيْعُ.
Chapter of invalid sale. And if one combines between a slave and a mudabbar, or between his own slave and another's slave, it is valid for the slave for his portion of the price. Jabir said: The Prophet sold a mudabbar. And the Prophet forbade najsh (bidding up) and forbade bidding over a brother's bid. Ibn Umar said: The Prophet forbade najsh. Abu Hurairah said: The Prophet forbade that a town dweller sell for a Bedouin, and do not engage in najsh, and do not outbid a brother's sale, and do not propose over a brother's proposal, and a woman shall not ask for the divorce of her sister to consume what is in her vessel. And regarding meeting traders and a town dweller selling for a Bedouin. Abu Hurairah said: The Prophet forbade meeting (traders) and that a town dweller sell for a Bedouin. And regarding sale at the call of Friday prayer. Allah said: O you who believe, when the call is made for prayer on Friday, hasten to the remembrance of Allah and leave off sale. All that is disliked and the sale is not invalidated by it.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage from al-Quduri's matn presents a series of rulings on prohibited practices in sales that nonetheless do not affect the validity of the sale contract. The author first gives a ruling on combining a slave with a mudabbar or with another's slave in a single sale: the sale is valid for the slave for his portion of the price, supported by a hadith where the Prophet sold a mudabbar. Then he lists several forbidden practices: najsh (artificially inflating price by feigning interest), bidding over a brother's bid (interfering in a negotiation), meeting traders before they reach the market (to buy cheap and resell), a town dweller selling for a Bedouin (acting as a middleman without the Bedouin's knowledge), and selling at the time of Friday adhan. Each practice is supported by ahadith or Quranic verse. The key legal point is that all these are makruh (disliked) but the sale itself remains valid (la yafsudu). The Hanafi position distinguishes between prohibitions that affect the essence of the contract (which would invalidate) and those external to it (which only render the act sinful but the contract sound). The ahadith serve as evidence for the prohibition, but since the prohibition is not directed at the essential elements (offer, acceptance, subject matter), the sale is not corrupted.

Key Terms
النَّجْش (al-najsh): Artificially inflating the price of an item by feigning interest or making a false bid without intending to buy.
السَّوْمَ عَلَى سَوْمٍ أَخِيهِ (al-sawm 'ala sawmi akhihi): Bidding or negotiating over a price after another buyer has already begun negotiating with the seller.
تَلَقِّي الْجَلَبِ (talaqqi al-jalab): Meeting traders bringing goods to market before they arrive, in order to buy at a low price and sell at a profit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person sells his slave and another person's slave in one contract for a lump sum, what is the ruling?
A) The sale is entirely invalid (batil).
B) The sale is valid only for the buyer's own slave.
C) The sale is valid for the buyer's slave for his portion of the price.
D) The sale is suspended (mawquf) until the seller acquires the other slave.

Key Takeaway
External prohibitions in a sale (like najsh, talaqqi) do not corrupt the contract; the sale remains valid but the act is sinful.

Ruling on Separating Minor Slaves Who Are Mahram Relatives
States ruling on separating minor slave relatives in sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling (makruh tanzihi, sale valid) for separating minor mahram slaves and apply it to scenarios involving adults.

Core Idea
Separating minor mahram slaves is disliked but the sale remains valid; for adults no objection.

This passage from al-Quduri's matn addresses a specific case in slave sales: what if the two slaves are related as mahram? The Hanafi school balances the harm of separation against the validity of the contract. Let's explore the three scenarios and the prophetic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its scenarios
The act is selling two slaves who are mahram relatives. Three scenarios: both minors, one adult one minor, both adults. Identify the ruling for each.
Mahram: relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For both minors: do not separate (strongly disliked). For one adult one minor: separation is disliked (makruh) but sale valid. For both adults: no objection (la ba'sa).
Karahah is tanzihi (light dislike) unless specified otherwise.
3
Locate the hadith proofs
First hadith: threat of separation from loved ones on Qiyamah for separating mother and child. Second hadith: Prophet ordered Ali to return a brother he sold separately.
Both hadith are ahad (single-chain) and zanni al-thubut.
4
Understand the wajh al-dalalah (indication)
The threat indicates strong discouragement (karahah) but does not nullify the contract. Hanafi usul: such threats imply karahah, not haram, because they do not explicitly invalidate the sale.
Compare with other schools that interpret the threat as prohibition.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Imam Ahmad holds separation is haram and sale invalid. Hanafi school holds it is makruh and sale valid. The practical difference (thamarah) is the validity of the sale.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different usul principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ مَلَكَ مَمْلُوكَيْنِ صَغِيرَيْنِ أَحَدُهُمَا ذُو رَحِمٍ مَحْرَمٍ مِنْ الْآخَرِ لَمْ يُفَرِّقْ بَيْنَهُمَا وَكَذَلِكَ لَوْ كَانَ أَحَدُهُمَا كَبِيرًا، وَالْآخَرُ صَغِيرًا فَإِنْ فَرَّقَ بَيْنَهُمَا كُرِهَ لَهُ ذَلِكَ وَجَازَ الْبَيْعُ فَإِنْ كَانَا كَبِيرَيْنِ فَلَا بَأْسَ بِالتَّفْرِيقِ بَيْنَهُمَا. عَنْ أَبِي أَيُّوبَ قَالَ: سَمِعْتُ رَسُولَ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم يَقُولُ: «مَنْ فَرَّقَ بَيْنَ الْوَالِدَةِ وَوَلَدِهَا فَرَّقَ اللَّهُ بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ أَحِبَّتِهِ يَوْمَ القِيَامَةِ» (سنن الترمذي، 1283). عَنْ عَلِيَّ قَالَ: وَهَبَ لِي رَسُولُ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم غُلَامَيْنِ أَخَوَيْنِ فَبِعْتُ أَحَدَهُمَا، فَقَالَ لِي رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «يَا عَلِيُّ مَا فَعَلَ غُلَامُكَ»، فَأَخْبَرْتُهُ، فَقَالَ: «رُدَّهُ رُدَّهُ» (سنن الترمذي، 1284).
And whoever owns two minor slaves, one of whom is a mahram relative of the other, he shall not separate between them. And likewise if one of them is adult and the other minor, if he separates between them it is disliked for him and the sale is permissible. And if both are adults, there is no harm in separating between them. From Abu Ayyub: I heard the Messenger of Allah say: 'Whoever separates a mother from her child, Allah will separate him from his loved ones on the Day of Resurrection.' From Ali: The Messenger of Allah gave me two brothers (slaves), and I sold one of them. He asked me about him, and I told him. He said: 'Return him, return him.'
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage from al-Quduri's matn addresses the ruling on separating slave relatives when selling them. The general principle is that it is disliking (makruh) to separate minor slaves who are mahram relatives (e.g., mother and child, siblings) from each other. However, the sale itself is valid if separation occurs. The ruling distinguishes three scenarios: (1) both are minors: separation is strongly disliked; (2) one adult and one minor: separation is still disliked but permissible; (3) both adults: no objection to selling them separately. The proofs include a hadith threatening separation from loved ones in the afterlife for separating mother and child, and the incident of Ali where the Prophet ordered him to return a brother he had sold separately. The Hanafi school takes these as discouragement but not prohibition of the sale itself. The underlying principle (as stated in the usul note) is that causing harm to the slaves is to be avoided, but the contract is not invalidated because the harm is not a defect in the sale's essentials.

Key Terms
ذُو رَحِمٍ مَحْرَمٍ (dhu rahim mahram): A relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited (e.g., mother, sister, daughter).
التَّفْرِيقُ بَيْنَ الْمَمْلُوكِينَ (al-tafriq bayna al-mamlookain): Selling two slaves separately when they are mahram relatives, causing separation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a person separates two adult slave brothers by selling them to different buyers?
A) It is haram and the sale is invalid.
B) It is makruh but the sale is valid.
C) There is no objection (la ba'sa) and the sale is permissible.
D) It is only permissible if both consent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, separating minor mahram slaves is makruh, not haram, and the sale remains valid; for adults, no restriction.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

8 min
Ruling on Iqaalah (Annulment of Sale)
Text and proofs for the ruling on annulment of sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid iqaalah and explain its legal nature according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Iqaalah is valid only with the exact original price; it is a rescission between parties but a new sale for others.

Iqaalah, or mutual rescission of a sale, is a permissible practice in Islamic commercial law. This passage from the classical Hanafi text clarifies when and how iqaalah is valid—specifically that it must involve the exact original price, and any deviation invalidates the condition. We will explore the proofs from hadith and companion statements, and understand the nuanced Hanafi position on its legal effect for the parties and third parties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its core ruling
The passage states that iqaalah is permissible (ja'iz) in a sale, but only when conducted with the same price as the original. Any condition of increase or decrease makes the condition void.
Focus on the phrase 'بِمِثْلِ الثَّمَنِ الْأَوَّلِ'.
2
Examine the conditions for validity
Note the specific conditions: destruction of the price does not prevent iqaalah, but destruction of the sold item does. If part of the item is destroyed, iqaalah is valid for the remainder.
Distinguish between halak al-thaman and halak al-mabi'.
3
Analyze the legal nature according to Abu Hanifah
Abu Hanifah rules that iqaalah is a rescission (faskh) between the parties, but a new sale (bay' jadid) regarding third parties. This affects rights like pre-emption.
Compare with the Shafi'i view that it is a new sale in all respects.
4
Review the proofs
The text cites a hadith urging iqaalah for a Muslim, and athar from Ibn Abbas and al-Aswad disapproving of any extra payment upon annulment. These support the condition of exact price.
The hadith is primarily a virtue (fadl), not a legal obligation; permissibility is from general principles.
5
Consider the practical application
Apply the ruling to a scenario: a buyer returns a car for a full refund—valid iqaalah. If the seller accepts a deduction, it becomes a new sale, not iqaalah.
The distinction affects whether the contract is rescinded ab initio or a new transaction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَابُ الْإِقَالَةِ
قَالَ - رَحِمَهُ اللهُ -: الْإِقَالَةُ جَائِزَةٌ فِي الْبَيْعِ بِمِثْلِ الثَّمَنِ الْأَوَّلِ، فَإِنْ شَرَطَ أَكْثَرَ مِنْهُ، أَوْ أَقَلَّ فَالشَّرْطُ بَاطِلٌ. وَهِيَ فَسْخُ فِي حَقِّ الْمُتَعَاقِدَيْنِ، بَيْعٌ جَدِيدٌ فِي حَقِّ غَيْرِهِمَا فِي قَوْلِ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ. وَهَلَاكُ الثَّمَنِ لَا يَمْنَعُ صِحَّةَ الْإِقَالَةِ، وَهَلَاكُ الْمَبِيعِ يَمْنَعُ مِنْهَا. وَإِنْ هَلَكَ بَعْضُ الْمَبِيعِ جَازَتْ الْإِقَالَةُ فِي بَاقِيهِ.
وَجْهٌ (١) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ الْإِقَالَةِ جَائِزَةٌ فِي الْبَيْعِ بِمِثْلِ الثَّمَنِ الْأَوَّلِ: عَنْ أَبِي هُرَيْرَةَ، قَالَ: قَالَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «مَنْ أَقَالَ مُسْلِمًا أَقَالَهُ اللهُ عَشْرَتَهُ». (سنن أبي داود، بَابٌ فِي فَضْلِ الْإِقَالَةِ، رقم 3460؛ سنن ابن ماجه، بَابُ الْإِقَالَةِ، رقم 2199)
وَجْهٌ (٢) قَوْلُ الصَّحَابِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ الْإِقَالَةِ جَائِزَةٌ فِي الْبَيْعِ بِمِثْلِ الثَّمَنِ الْأَوَّلِ: عَنِ ابْنِ عَبَّاسٍ أَنَّهُ كَرِهَ أَنْ يَبْتَاعَ الْبَيْعَ ثُمَّ يَرُدَّهُ وَيَرُدَّ مَعَهُ دَرَاهِمَ. وَفِي هَذَا دَلَالَةٌ عَلَى أَنَّ الْإِقَالَةَ فَسْخٌ، فَلَا تَجُوزُ إِلَّا بِرَأْسِ الْمَالِ. (السنن الكبرى للبيهقي، بَابُ مَنْ أَقَالَ الْمُسْلَمَ إِلَيْهِ بَعْضَ السَّلَمِ وَقَبَضَ بَعْضًا، رقم 11133)
وَجْهٌ (٣) قَوْلُ التَّابِعِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ الْإِقَالَةِ جَائِزَةٌ فِي الْبَيْعِ بِمِثْلِ الثَّمَنِ الْأَوَّلِ: عَنِ الْأَسْوَدِ، أَنَّهُ كَرِهَ أَنْ يَرُدَّهَا، وَيَرُدَّ مَعَهَا شَيْئًا، هَذَا فِي الَّذِي يَشْتَرِي السَّلْعَةَ، فَيَقُولُ: «أَقِلْنِي وَلَكَ كَذَا وَكَذَا». (مصنف عبد الرزاق، بَابُ الرَّجُلُ يَشْتَرِي السَّلْعَةَ فَيَقُولُ: أَقِلْنِي وَلَكَ كَذَا، رقم 14131)
وَجْهٌ (أ) قَوْلُ الصَّحَابِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ فَإِنْ شَرَطَ أَكْثَرَ مِنْهُ، أَوْ أَقَلَّ فَالشَّرْطُ بَاطِلٌ: عَنِ ابْنِ عَبَّاسٍ أَنَّهُ كَرِهَ أَنْ يَبْتَاعَ الْبَيْعَ ثُمَّ يَرُدَّهُ وَيَرُدَّ مَعَهُ دَرَاهِمَ. وَفِي هَذَا دَلَالَةٌ عَلَى أَنَّ الْإِقَالَةَ فَسْخٌ، فَلَا تَجُوزُ إِلَّا بِرَأْسِ الْمَالِ. (السنن الكبرى للبيهقي، بَابُ مَنْ أَقَالَ الْمُسْلَمَ إِلَيْهِ بَعْضَ السَّلَمِ وَقَبَضَ بَعْضًا، رقم 11133)
اصول : اقالہ بیچ اول کا فسخ ہے، بیع مکمل ہونے کے بعد کسی مجبوری سے مشتری ثمن واپس کر کے مبیع لے لینا اقالہ کہلاتا ہے، اور یہ جائز ہے۔
اصول : مبیع میں کمی یا زیادتی ہو جائے تو اقالہ جائز نہیں ہے۔
اصول : خلاف جنس ثمن کے ساتھ اقالہ کرنا جائز نہیں ہے، کیونکہ ایسی صورت میں ثمن اول پر فسخ نہ ہوا۔
اصول : اقالہ میں اصل واپسی مبیع کی ہوتی ہے، ثمن کی نہیں۔
Chapter of Iqaalah. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Iqaalah is valid in sale with the same price as the first. If one stipulates more or less, the condition is void. It is a rescission regarding the two contracting parties, a new sale regarding others according to Abu Hanifah. Destruction of the price does not prevent validity of iqaalah; destruction of the sold item prevents it. If part of the sold item is destroyed, iqaalah is valid for the remainder. Proof 1: Hadith... Proof 2: Statement of Ibn Abbas... Proof 3: Statement of al-Aswad... Proof (a): Statement of Ibn Abbas... Principles: Iqaalah is rescission of the first sale...
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on iqaalah (annulment of sale). It belongs to the category of ruling with proof. The author states that iqaalah is permitted only when the purchase price is returned exactly, without any increase or decrease. Any condition to the contrary renders the stipulation invalid. Then the author explains the legal nature of iqaalah: according to Abu Hanifah, it is a rescission (faskh) between the parties, but for third parties (e.g., subsequent buyers or creditors) it is treated as a new sale. This distinction has practical implications, for example regarding rights of pre-emption. The text then lists conditions: destruction of the price does not prevent iqaalah, but destruction of the sold item prevents it. If only part is destroyed, iqaalah is valid for the remainder. The proofs include a hadith urging the practice, and athar from Ibn Abbas and al-Aswad indicating disapproval of any excess payment upon annulment. The Urdu principles summarize key points: iqaalah is rescission of the original sale, not permissible if goods have changed, not permissible with something other than the original price, and the return of the sold item is primary.

Key Terms
الْإِقَالَة (al-iqālah): Annulment of a sale by mutual consent, returning the goods and price.
فَسْخ (faskh): Rescission; cancellation of the contract ab initio.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Iqaalah is invalid entirely.
B) Iqaalah is valid only for the remaining part.
C) Iqaalah is valid only if the buyer pays extra.
D) Iqaalah is valid only if the seller agrees.

Key Takeaway
Iqaalah is a rescission of the original sale, valid only when the exact price is returned; any deviation makes the condition void.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Rulings on Murabaha and Tawliya
Definitions and conditions for cost-plus and transfer-at-cost sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definitions of murabaha and tawliya, identify the condition of fungibility (mithli) for validity, list permissible expenses that may be added to cost, and explain the buyer's options when fraud is discovered.

Core Idea
Murabaha and tawliya are valid only for fungible goods; fraud in murabaha gives an option to rescind, while in tawliya it allows price deduction.

This lesson deals with two special types of sale: murabaha (cost-plus) and tawliya (transfer at cost). The Hanafi school lays down specific conditions, most importantly that the goods must be fungible (mithli). You will also learn how expenses may be included in the cost statement and what happens if the seller is dishonest about the original price.

Guided Path
1
Define murabaha and tawliya
Read the definitions: murabaha is selling at cost plus a stated profit; tawliya is selling at the original cost without profit. Note that both require disclosure of the original purchase price.
The key difference is the presence or absence of profit.
2
Identify the condition for validity
The passage states that murabaha and tawliya are not valid unless the countervalue (subject matter) is mithli – an item that has an exact equivalent, like measured or weighed goods. Consider why this condition exists: the buyer relies on the seller's word regarding cost, and fungible goods have a known market value.
Unique, non-fungible items (e.g., real estate) are not suitable for these contracts.
3
List permissible expenses that may be added to capital
The seller may include expenses such as wages of a fuller, dyer, embroiderer, or transport cost. He must phrase it as 'it cost me such-and-such' and not claim that he bought it for that price. Distinguish between expenses that add value and those that do not.
This avoids deception (gharar) about the actual cost.
4
Explain the buyer's remedy upon discovering fraud
For murabaha, Abu Hanifah gives the buyer a choice: accept at the stated total price or return the goods. For tawliya, the buyer simply deducts the fraudulent amount from the price. Understand the reason for the difference: in tawliya there is no profit, so the buyer is entitled to the correct cost.
Fraud (khiyanah) undermines the trust that these contracts require.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَابُ الْمُرَابَحَةِ وَالتَّوْلِيَةِ
قَالَ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ: الْمُرَابَحَةُ نَقْلُ مَا مَلَكَهُ بِالْعَقْدِ الْأَوَّلِ بِالثَّمَنِ الْأَوَّلِ مَعَ زِيَادَةِ رِبْحٍ، وَالتَّوْلِيَةُ نَقْلُ مَا مَلَكَهُ بِالْعَقْدِ الْأَوَّلِ بِالثَّمَنِ الْأَوَّلِ مِنْ غَيْرِ زِيَادَةِ رِبْحٍ. وَلَا تَصِحُّ الْمُرَابَحَةُ وَلَا التَّوْلِيَةُ حَتَّى يَكُونَ الْعِوَضُ مِمَّا لَهُ مِثْلٌ كَالْمَكِيلِ، وَالْمَوْزُونِ. وَيَجُوزُ أَنْ يُضِيفَ إِلَى رَأْسِ الْمَالِ أُجْرَةَ الْقَصَّارِ وَالصَّبَّاغِ وَالطَّرَّازِ، وَالْفَتَالِ وَأُجْرَةَ حَمْلِ الطَّعَامِ، وَيَقُولُ: قَامَ عَلَيَّ بِكَذَا، وَلَا يَقُولُ: اشْتَرَيْتُهُ بِكَذَا. وَإِذَا اطَّلَعَ الْمُشْتَرِي عَلَى خِيَانَةٍ فِي الْمُرَابَحَةِ فَهُوَ بِالْخِيَارِ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ: إِنْ شَاءَ أَخَذَهُ بِجَمِيعِ الثَّمَنِ، وَإِنْ شَاءَ رَدَّهُ. وَإِنِ اطَّلَعَ عَلَى خِيَانَةٍ فِي التَّوْلِيَةِ أَسْقَطَهَا مِنَ الثَّمَنِ.
وَجْهٌ (١) قَوْلُ التَّابِعِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ الْمُرَابَحَةِ نَقْلُ مَا مَلَكَهُ بِالْعَقْدِ الْأَوَّلِ بِالثَّمَنِ الْأَوَّلِ: قَالَ: رَأَيْتُ عَلَى عَلِيٍّ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُ إِزَارًا غَلِيظًا، قَالَ: اشْتَرَيْتُ بِخَمْسَةِ دَرَاهِمَ فَمَنْ أَرْبَحَنِي فِيهِ دِرْهَمًا بِعْتُهُ إِيَّاهُ. (السنن الكبرى للبيهقي، بَابُ الْمُرَابَحَةِ، رقم 10794)
وَجْهٌ (٢) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ الْمُرَابَحَةِ نَقْلُ مَا مَلَكَهُ بِالْعَقْدِ الْأَوَّلِ بِالثَّمَنِ الْأَوَّلِ: قَالَتْ عَائِشَةُ: فَبَيْنَمَا نَحْنُ يَوْمًا جُلُوسٌ فِي بَيْتِ أَبِي بَكْرٍ ... فَقَالَ أَبُو بَكْرٍ: فَخُذْ بِأَبِي أَنْتَ يَا رَسُولَ اللَّهِ إِحْدَى رَاحِلَتَيَّ هَاتَيْنِ، قَالَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «بِالثَّمَنِ». (صحيح البخاري، بَابُ هِجْرَةِ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم وَأَصْحَابِهِ إِلَى الْمَدِينَةِ، رقم 3905)
وَجْهٌ (١) قَوْلُ التَّابِعِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ وَيَجُوزُ أَنْ يُضِيفَ إِلَى رَأْسِ الْمَالِ أُجْرَةَ الْقَصَّارِ: قُلْتُ لِإِبْرَاهِيمَ إِنَّا نَشْتَرِي الْمَتَاعَ ثُمَّ نَزِيدُ عَلَيْهِ الْقَصَّارَةَ وَالْكِرَاءَ، ثُمَّ نَبِيعُهُ بِدَهْيَازُدَهُ، قَالَ: «لَا بَأْسَ». (مصنف ابن أبي شيبة، في النَّفَقَةِ تُضَمُّ إِلَى رَأْسِ الْمَالِ، رقم 20411)
وَجْهٌ (أ) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ وَإِذَا اطَّلَعَ الْمُشْتَرِي عَلَى خِيَانَةٍ فِي الْمُرَابَحَةِ: عَنْ أَبِي ذَرٍّ، عَنِ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «ثَلَاثَةٌ لَا يَنْظُرُ اللَّهُ إِلَيْهِمْ يَوْمَ الْقِيَامَةِ، وَلَا يُزَكِّيهِمْ، وَلَهُمْ عَذَابٌ أَلِيمٌ»، قُلْنَا: مَنْ هُمْ يَا رَسُولَ اللَّهِ، فَقَدْ خَابُوا وَخَسِرُوا فَقَالَ: «الْمَنَّانُ، وَالْمُسْبِلُ إِزَارَهُ، وَالْمُنَفِّقُ سِلْعَتَهُ بِالْخَلِفِ الْكَاذِبِ». (سنن الترمذي، بَابُ مَا جَاءَ فِيمَنْ حَلَفَ عَلَى سِلْعَةٍ كَاذِبًا، رقم 1211)
اصول : ثمن اول میں نفع کیساتھ مبیع کو بیچنا مرابحہ ، اور ثمن اول پر ہی مبیع کو بیچنا تولیہ ہے ، اور دونوں جائز ہے۔
Chapter of Murabaha and Tawliya. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Murabaha is transferring what one owns by the first contract with the first price along with an increase of profit. Tawliya is transferring what one owns by the first contract with the first price without an increase of profit. Murabaha and tawliya are not valid unless the countervalue is of a type that has an equivalent, like measured or weighed items. It is permissible to add to the capital the wages of the fuller, dyer, embroiderer, wick-twister, and the carriage cost of food, and he should say: 'It cost me such-and-such' and not say: 'I bought it for such-and-such.' When the buyer discovers fraud in murabaha, he has a choice according to Abu Hanifah: if he wishes, he may take it for the entire price; if he wishes, he may return it. If he discovers fraud in tawliya, he deducts it from the price...
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage treats the legal rules for two types of sale: murabaha (cost-plus sale) and tawliya (transfer at cost). The author first defines them: in murabaha, the seller sells an item for its purchase price plus a stated profit; in tawliya, he sells it for the original purchase price without profit. These sales are only valid when the item is fungible (mithli), such as items sold by weight or measure — because the buyer relies on the seller's statement of cost, and fungibles have a known market equivalent. The seller may include certain expenses (like tailoring, dyeing, transport) in the declared cost, but he must phrase it as 'it cost me' rather than 'I bought it for', to avoid lying. If the buyer discovers that the seller has overstated the cost (fraud), Abu Hanifah gives the buyer an option in murabaha: either accept at the stated total price or return the goods. In tawliya, since no profit is involved, the buyer simply deducts the fraudulent amount from the price. The proofs include a report from Ali offering a profit, a hadith about Abu Bakr selling a mount to the Prophet at cost, a statement from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i permitting inclusion of expenses, and a hadith warning against false oaths in sale. The Urdu principle reiterates that murabaha and tawliya are permissible.

Key Terms
الْمُرَابَحَة (al-murābaḥah): A sale where the seller states the original cost and adds a known profit.
التَّوْلِيَة (al-tawliyah): A sale where the seller transfers the item at the original cost without profit.
خِيَانَة (khiyānah): Fraud or dishonesty in disclosing the true cost of the item.
مِثْلِي (mithlī): Fungible: an item that has exact equivalents in the market, like grains or liquids sold by weight or measure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the buyer's remedy in tawliya if he discovers that the true cost was less than stated?
A) He may return the goods.
B) He may deduct the overcharge from the price.
C) He may keep the goods without payment.
D) He has no remedy.

Key Takeaway
Murabaha and tawliya are permissible sales, but only for fungible goods, and fraud gives the buyer an option (murabaha) or a deduction (tawliya).

Murabahah and Tawliyah: Definitions and the Mithli Condition
Defines two sales and mandates mithli commodity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of Murabahah and Tawliyah, identify the condition of mithli for validity, and explain the proofs cited by al-Quduri.

Core Idea
Murabahah and Tawliyah require the commodity to have a standard equivalent — otherwise the cost cannot be known.

This lesson introduces two essential sale contracts: Murabahah (cost-plus-profit) and Tawliyah (sale at cost). Al-Quduri defines them precisely and then imposes a critical condition: the commodity must be of a type that has a like (mithl), such as measured or weighed goods. Understanding this condition is key to grasping the Hanafi rationale for these contracts and their distinction from the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Parse the definitions
Identify the core elements of each contract: transfer at the first price, with (Murabahah) or without (Tawliyah) an added profit.
2
Isolate the condition
The text states that neither contract is valid unless the countervalue (al-iwad) is of a type that has a mithl, exemplified by makil (measured) and mawzun (weighed) items.
Mithli goods are those with standard equivalents; unique items like cars or paintings are not mithli.
3
Examine the proofs
Two proofs are cited: (1) the statement of a Tabi'i regarding Ali (radiallahu anhu) buying an izār and offering profit, and (2) the hadith of Abu Bakr offering a riding beast to the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) 'for the price', implying permission to sell at cost or with profit.
The proofs establish the permissibility of these contracts in principle.
4
Differentiate from the Shafi'i school
The Hanafi school requires the commodity to be mithli; the Shafi'i school does not. The ikhtilaf stems from whether accurate knowledge of cost can be achieved without standard measures.
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is the condition of mithli.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَابُ الْمُرَابَحَةِ وَالتَّوْلِيَةِ
قَالَ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ: الْمُرَابَحَةُ نَقْلُ مَا مَلَكَهُ بِالْعَقْدِ الْأَوَّلِ بِالثَّمَنِ الْأَوَّلِ مَعَ زِيَادَةِ رِبْحٍ، وَالتَّوْلِيَةُ نَقْلُ مَا مَلَكَهُ بِالْعَقْدِ الْأَوَّلِ بِالثَّمَنِ الْأَوَّلِ مِنْ غَيْرِ زِيَادَةِ رِبْحٍ. وَلَا تَصِحُ الْمُرَابَحَةُ وَلَا التَّوْلِيَةُ حَتَّى يَكُونَ الْعِوَضُ مِمَّا لَهُ مِثْلٌ كَالْمَكِيلِ وَالْمَوْزُونِ.
وَجْهٌ (١) قَوْلُ التَّابِعِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ الْمُرَابَحَةِ: قَالَ: رَأَيْتُ عَلَى عَلِيٍّ إِزَارًا غَلِيظًا، قَالَ: اشْتَرَيْتُ بِخَمْسَةِ دَرَاهِمَ فَمَنْ أَرْبَحَنِي فِيهِ دِرْهَمًا بِعْتُهُ إِيَّاهُ. (السنن الكبرى للبيهقي، باب المرابحة، رقم 10794)
وَجْهٌ (٢) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ الْمُرَابَحَةِ: قَالَتْ عَائِشَةُ: فَبَيْنَمَا نَحْنُ يَوْمًا جُلُوسٌ فِي بَيْتِ أَبِي بَكْرٍ ... فَقَالَ أَبُو بَكْرٍ: بِأَبِي أَنْتَ يَا رَسُولَ اللَّهِ؟ قَالَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ: نَعَمْ. قَالَ أَبُو بَكْرٍ: فَخُذْ بِأَبِي أَنْتَ يَا رَسُولَ اللَّهِ إِحْدَى رَاحِلَتَيَّ هَاتَيْنِ، قَالَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ: بِالثَّمَنِ. (صحيح بخاري، باب هجرة النبي وأصحابه إلى المدينة، رقم 3905)
أُصُولٌ: ثَمَنِ أَوَّلٍ مَعَ نَفْعٍ كَيْسَاتٍ مَبِيعٌ کُو بَيْچْنَا مُرَابَحَةً، اور ثَمَنِ اَوَّلٍ پَرْ ہی مَبِيعٌ کُو بَيْچْنَا تَوْلِيَةً ہے، اور دونوں جَائِزٌ ہے.
Chapter of Murabahah and Tawliyah. He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: Murabahah is transferring what one owns by the first contract at the first price with an increase of profit, and Tawliyah is transferring what one owns by the first contract at the first price without an increase of profit. Murabahah and Tawliyah are not valid unless the countervalue is of a type that has a like, such as measured and weighed items. Proof (1): Statement of the Tabi'i to establish Murabahah: He said: I saw on Ali a coarse wrapper; he said: I bought it for five dirhams, so whoever gives me a dirham profit in it, I will sell it to him. (al-Sunan al-Kubra al-Bayhaqi, chapter of Murabahah, number 10794) Proof (2): Hadith to establish Murabahah: Aisha said: One day while we were sitting in the house of Abu Bakr... Abu Bakr said: May my father be ransomed for you, O Messenger of Allah? The Messenger of Allah said: Yes. Abu Bakr said: Take, may my father be ransomed for you, O Messenger of Allah, one of these two riding beasts of mine. The Messenger of Allah said: For the price. (Sahih al-Bukhari, chapter of the Hijrah of the Prophet and his Companions to Madinah, number 3905) Principle: Selling the commodity with profit on the first price is Murabahah, and selling the commodity exactly at the first price is Tawliyah, and both are permissible.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a definition_ruling from the matn of al-Quduri. It defines two types of sales: Murabahah (cost-plus-profit sale) and Tawliyah (sale at cost). Both are valid only if the commodity belongs to the category of mithliyyat—items that have standard equivalents, such as measured (makil) and weighed (mawzun) goods. This condition ensures that the cost can be accurately determined. The passage then provides two proofs: the first is a statement from a Tabi'i (follower) reporting that Ali (radiallahu anhu) engaged in Murabahah. The second is a hadith from Aisha (radiallahu anha) about Abu Bakr offering a camel to the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) at a price, implying the permissibility of selling at a profit. The Urdu summary at the end reiterates the definitions.

Key Terms
الْمُرَابَحَةُ (al-murābaḥah): A sale where the seller discloses the original cost and adds a specified profit.
التَّوْلِيَةُ (al-tawliyah): A sale where the seller transfers the commodity at the original cost without any profit.
مِثْلٌ (mithl): A commodity that has standard equivalents in the market, such as measured (makil) or weighed (mawzun) goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The commodity must be food.
B) The commodity must be a luxury item.
C) The commodity must be of a type that has a like, such as measured or weighed items.
D) The commodity must be purchased by the seller himself.

Key Takeaway
Murabahah and Tawliyah are valid only if the commodity has a standard equivalent (mithl), ensuring the cost is known and dispute-free.
